Broadcom CEO on the Biggest AI Chip Bets

Bloomberg Technology(727.0K)·18:47·2.2K views·2026-06-05
Hock TanPresident & CEO, Broadcom Tom GilesReporter, Bloomberg
TL;DRBroadcom is prioritizing massive organic growth over mergers and acquisitions, leveraging custom AI silicon partnerships with Google and OpenAI to drive record-breaking revenues.
CEO Hock Tan remains focused on "out-engineering" competitors and capturing insatiable...

TL;DR

Broadcom is prioritizing massive organic growth over mergers and acquisitions, leveraging custom AI silicon partnerships with Google and OpenAI to drive record-breaking revenues. CEO Hock Tan remains focused on "out-engineering" competitors and capturing insatiable enterprise demand for AI networking and compute infrastructure.

Custom Silicon & High-Profile Partnerships

  • Google Co-Development: Broadcom maintains a deep partnership with Google to co-design Tensor Processing Units (TPUs), which are deployed via Google Cloud.
      • Customer-Owned Tooling (COT): Tan downplays Google's efforts to design chips in-house with smaller partners like Marvell and MediaTek, referring to them as "ankle biters."
      • The Nvidia Benchmark: Broadcom’s goal is to help Google build TPUs that match the generational performance of Nvidia's market-leading GPUs.
  • OpenAI Silicon Timeline: Broadcom's custom AI accelerator silicon designed for OpenAI is already running in data centers and labs, and is on track for production in late 2026.
      • No Contract Friction: Tan dismissed rumors that the partnership is facing delays or requires Microsoft to guarantee chip purchases.
  • Selective Customer Strategy: Broadcom limits its custom AI silicon program to only six strategic customers, all of whom are at various stages of designing proprietary hardware.

Enterprise AI, Anthropic, and Productivity ROI

  • The Anthropic Bet: Broadcom and Google took a "leap of faith" a year ago to supply compute capacity to Anthropic via co-designed TPUs, a move Tan says has paid off spectacularly as Anthropic targets enterprise tools.
  • Dramatic Engineering ROI: Broadcom uses tools like Anthropic’s Opus internally for engineering design and code assistance, reporting remarkable productivity returns.
      • Efficiency Metric: A senior engineer using AI tools can complete an application design in one week that would historically take 10 engineers (paid $300,000 annually) three months to complete.
  • Token Spend and Costs: Tan believes enterprise concerns over token costs are overblown if the return on investment remains high, noting that enterprise AI adoption is still in its "early innings."

Shifting Away from Historic M&A Strategy

  • Organic Growth Surge: Broadcom is on track to double its annualized revenues to over $100 billion, adding $50 billion in organic revenue between 2024 and 2026.
  • M&A as a Distraction: Tan states that because of insatiable demand for generative AI "picks and shovels," pursuing acquisitions is currently a distraction due to regulatory and integration friction.
  • Avoiding "Bright Shiny Objects": Broadcom will not seek exceptions to its M&A pause for emerging fields like photonics, preferring to stick to its disciplined, core semiconductor model.

AI Networking Dominance and Cisco Competition

  • Infrastructure Leadership: Out of Broadcom's portfolio of 17 semiconductor divisions, 5 to 6 are dedicated to AI clusters, with switching gear and networking "taking off like a rocket."
  • Managing Demand Overflow: While legacy competitors like Cisco are attempting to enter the AI networking space, Broadcom is prioritizing strategic, long-term customers and is comfortable letting the overflow demand go to competitors.

Notable Quotes

"Customer-owned tooling that Google is trying to create, which is trying to design it themselves, with the help of perhaps some smaller partners—Marvell maybe, MediaTek—I call them kind of like ankle biters... We just compete against my own customer in a different part of it and the whole idea is to be able to create differentiated product and technology that beats what they have." — Hock Tan
"In the last two years, between '24 and '26 this year, I would be doubling my revenues. I would create over $50 billion per year, annualized, on revenue. I'm looking around, what can I buy? They don't even come close to that. And that's the tricky part... Organically, this phenomenon we call generative AI and our ability to ship them picks and shovels... makes it very hard to choose M&A over focusing and succeeding." — Hock Tan
"You can get one very senior engineer to produce an application design in one week what it would otherwise take 10 engineers, which we pay at $300,000 a year, and take them three months to produce the same thing. The return on investment is still pretty compelling." — Hock Tan
Well, what does it say about the expectations that investors have placed on companies like yours, that you can release results reaffirming forecasts for $100 billion in certain types of revenue, surpass estimates in a lot of different ways, and still there to be this sense of disappointment? What does it say about where we are in the hype cycle around AI? I guess we're kind of like AI in a very surreal environment. Frankly, I don't think about it. Now, it's hard not to, but no—just focus on fundamentals, create value, and stop thinking about your stock price. The trouble is, it is very hard to do that, but we try to do it. Well, I mean as we were just talking with Ali, you know, every month or so, every couple of months there's a new LLM and it sort of changes the conversation around who's ahead, who's behind. I'm curious about, you know, you have this massive partnership with Google, very important working with them on their TPUs. I'd love to drill down a little bit with you on that, and in particular, how concerned you might be about Google sort of taking more of the chip development and design process in-house. Is the term "customer-owned tooling" something that keeps you up at night, and are there any provisions that you've built into your partnership with them that might preclude that? Well, you know, that's something we should expect in technology, and we've been in semiconductors for over 20 years. In fact, that's how long I've been doing this with Broadcom. That's what we do all the time, every year. We have multiple product divisions, in each of which we are the best at what we do, and we just keep investing. Essentially, you try to out-engineer any competition. Customer-owned tooling that Google is trying to create, which is trying to design it themselves with the help of perhaps some smaller partners—Marvell maybe, MediaTek, whom I call kind of like ankle biters—but they basically are working for Google to try to achieve customer-owned tooling. So we just compete against my own customer in a different part of it, and the whole idea is to be able to create a differentiated product and technology that beats what they have. And all this is very nice, but it's been helped by the fact that the competitor, the real competitor facing all this, is the GPU out of Nvidia. As long as Nvidia keeps coming up generation after generation with superb technology, Google has to create equivalent technology to match that. And that's where we come up. The TPU process, it's quite involved, and I'm wondering if there's anything Google, and anything you can do, to help Google democratize that process a little bit. How do you mean democratize? Just in the sense of, you know, making TPUs more accessible to mainstream developers and not just the elite AI labs. Well, I think Google is trying to do that by making available that TPU environment through the cloud—Google Cloud, GCP. But a big part of that is the software: the operating system, the software, and the compilers that go with it. And that's very much in Google's purview. Of course, we'd like to help, but I think they have that pretty much under control in terms of trying to push that program. I want to talk a little bit about Anthropic and the ways that these foundation model builders are obviously in great need of capital in order to finance their needs for chips and the compute that are needed to build and develop. Can you talk a little bit about the way that relationship is being structured and financed, and the provisions that you're having to take to kind of protect yourself against downside? How concerned are you about the end-user demand materializing and enabling them to meet their commitments? Frankly, I think in this particular case, we were partners with Google to provide compute capacity through TPUs, which we co-designed with Google, to Anthropic. And frankly, there was no shortcut about it when we first started, almost a year ago now, which was basically a leap of faith. We believed that Anthropic, generative AI, and Anthropic's business model of addressing enterprises with code assistance and coding tools would make a difference. We went in on that leap of faith, and I guess to date, that has been a great bet. They do seem to be in the ascent—very ascendant at the moment given the fact that they have leapfrogged ahead of OpenAI on their valuation, finally filing confidentially for an IPO. So have you not had to worry about, or have you not had conversations with investors who might be worried about you getting left holding the bag if things don't materialize the way Anthropic anticipates them? Well, as I said, we were making a bet on Anthropic and we've been—us and Google too—jointly doing that. And I guess we're not just making a bet on Anthropic and its business model, we are also making a bet that generative AI would be something that would take off, take off in a fairly substantial way, particularly among enterprises, the way Anthropic is going. And sure enough, it did, and there's a pretty good reason for it. Even though when it started a year ago I knew probably a fraction of what I know today, and what I know today is probably a fraction of what I'll know in six months' time. These things are changing very fast. But the tools we see coming out of a company like Anthropic, and the same applies to OpenAI and Gemini out of Google, they are just phenomenal in the sense of what they can achieve to help productivity. In Broadcom, we use them. We use those tools for engineering design and code assistance, and it's pretty remarkable what can be achieved in terms of enhancing productivity. More than enhancing productivity, I think they are even making product designs that are probably better than what we could do with just our engineers—and these are very good engineers that we have. Talk a little bit more about how you're advising your staff to use these different tools, and have you found yourself having to put limits on that? We hear about, you know, the era of token maxing—which I hate that term—but the idea of letting people just use tokens without limit. Have you had to put limits on that, and what does it look like? No, we don't. Frankly, on a bigger picture, we aren't at that stage yet. So maybe it's because we're behind other guys who have gotten to that point and started to put a throttle on what they do. Now, a big part of it is when you use a tool like Opus 4.7 and the engineers start using it, they go through a learning process of even how to use it. You can't get a tool to be very productive at the start, but as you keep using it and you use it long enough, you get very good at it. And then the tools become much more productive. As they get much more productive, just think about it: you can get one very senior engineer to produce an application design in one week that would otherwise take 10 engineers, whom we pay $300,000 a year, three months to produce. The return on investment is still pretty compelling. So to say we're throttling is probably a question of return on investment and what application you're using. But it is also about being trained to be able to use it better and better. Last year you announced a landmark agreement to work on AI chips with OpenAI. Could you give us an update on where things stand in kind of bringing that to fruition and, in particular, addressing what another publication wrote about maybe some snags there and your wanting Microsoft to agree to buy a certain percentage of the chips? Just how are things going with finalizing that agreement? Oh no, by the way, creating chips with partners—best example you said earlier, my great relationship with Google—while they try to do customer-owned tooling, it's very, very friendly in a way, and we all have the same goals: competition. We have a great and deep partnership with these guys, as we do with most of our other customers, as I call them. But it's a journey for them. So the first generation of chip would tend to be replaced by a second generation, which gets better and better. So for them, it's a journey towards creating custom AI accelerator silicon to basically optimize the workload to replace what is the de facto standard now, which is Nvidia. We do that, but we only have a few customers—to be exact, six. Each of them is on a journey at a different stage of the journey towards creating their own silicon which they will use. And obviously Google is way ahead, having started much earlier. OpenAI is one of them we have engaged with, and we've been engaged now for over two years. And it's pretty remarkable how much they have achieved because we have the AI accelerator silicon working very well in their labs, in their data centers, and we're on track to go into production late this year. And that's it—off we go to the races. So there's nothing holding back that partnership at this stage, whether it's needing Microsoft to make any kinds of agreements or anything like that? No, not at all. Great. I want to talk about M&A because historically Broadcom has used M&A in a relatively substantial way. Does the AI revolution sort of temper the need for you to supplement growth using mergers and acquisitions? Are you kind of entering a post-M&A phase, or is that the wrong way to think about it? No, you hit it right on, Tom. And it's an interesting question. It's a very valid question because it crosses my mind and that of my board on an ongoing basis—do we do M&A? So I'll be very flip and give you a straight answer. In the last two years, between '24 and '26, I will have doubled my revenues. I will create over $50 billion per year, annualized, in revenue. I'm looking around asking, what can I buy that even comes close to that? And that's the tricky part. I mean, it's a distraction. All M&As are a distraction: to acquire, to go through regulators, and a further distraction to integrate for another year. Meanwhile, organically, this phenomenon we call generative AI—and our ability to ship them picks and shovels, the compute capacity into this demand which is almost insatiable—makes it very hard to choose M&A over focusing and succeeding in generative AI compute. Is there any area where you think you might want to sort of make an exception to this? Whether it's maybe photonics, just to name one area. Photonics? Oh, you mean optics. We run a business—I've run this business model for 20 years. I try very hard—I know it's hard—to avoid bright, shiny objects. Alright. I want to ask you some of the same questions that I've asked some of the other keynote speakers. When you look at the price sensitivity around token usage and prioritizing spending on this, we've seen a lot of companies start to cut back or throttle their use of tokens, as we talked about earlier. We've also seen indications that certain Chinese large language models are starting to gain a lot of traction, particularly given how efficiently they can run—whether it's DeepSeek, Alibaba's Qwen, the list goes on. Are you seeing that at all from your vantage point? From what I've heard—I can't say we're doing it here in Broadcom—but what I've heard a lot of, and I assume you hear from the same sources, is a lot of company leaders, CIOs of companies, talking about, "Hey, you know, we're trying to find ways to reduce token costs and all that." And maybe they are ahead of where we are, but my sense of enterprise usage of AI is that we're still in the early innings of this game. And I think we're still trying to learn what we can get out of this phenomenal tool that we have created and continue to enhance and improve with each succeeding generation. So from my personal viewpoint, as I said earlier and from our experience running Broadcom, yeah, I don't want my guys to go nuts over spending on tokens. But on the other side, if you can create value out of it and get a return on investment that you otherwise could not, why stop? This is not like going to a video arcade when we were kids to play a game, and at the point when the monster is about to eat you and your gun runs out of ammunition, you feed in more tokens. It's almost like that, but not quite. I'd be remiss if we didn't talk about the other businesses that Broadcom is known for—networking in particular. You know, your gear has become increasingly important to the modern AI era. We're starting to see other companies like Cisco wanting to play in the areas that you've dominated, and I'm just curious what you think of the prospects for them to create competition for you there. Well, we're very fortunate—and I say that truthfully, not tongue-in-cheek. We've been in semiconductors for 20 years, and we've accumulated a portfolio of about 17 semiconductor product divisions in specific core areas where we are literally number one, or we're not in. We're number one, and we continue to invest. And about five or six of those divisions happen to be very important in creating AI chips and AI clusters. They all became that way, and one of them is networking, as you say, for switching gear. And that's taking off like a rocket. But we also try to be, as we always have been when we sell our products, very strategic. We look at a customer: are they using it for a particular purpose? Are they sustainable? Do they want not just one generation, but the next, and the next, and the next? And so we prioritize our products for those kinds of strategic customers. And demand, as I said, for AI and AI networking today is just massive. So it's okay for us; with our strength and the way we look at business, I'm happy for the overflow to go to Cisco.

CrowdStrike CEO George Kurtz goes one-on-one with Jim Cramer

CNBC Television(3.4M)·11:10·6.0K views·2026-06-05
George KurtzCEO, CrowdStrike Jim CramerHost, Mad Money
TL;DRDespite a minor post-earnings stock dip caused by lofty market expectations, CrowdStrike delivered strong Q1 results and raised its full-year ARR guidance by over $50 million, fueled by a 250% quarter-over-quarter surge in its AI pipeline.
Key Financial Metrics: ...

TL;DR

Despite a minor post-earnings stock dip caused by lofty market expectations, CrowdStrike delivered strong Q1 results and raised its full-year ARR guidance by over $50 million, fueled by a 250% quarter-over-quarter surge in its AI pipeline.

Financial Performance and Raised Outlook

  • Key Financial Metrics:
    • Achieved record new annual recurring revenue (ARR), accelerating revenue growth, and record free cash flow.
    • Posted a Rule of 40 score of 59, demonstrating elite-tier balance between growth and cash generation.
  • Raised Guidance:
    • Increased full-year net new ARR guidance by over $50 million, translating to a substantial 520 basis point raise.
    • The mid-April emergence of the "mythos" security vulnerability occurred too late in the quarter (ending April 30) to impact Q1 results but heavily bolsters the forward-looking pipeline.
  • Stock Split: Announced a 4-for-1 stock split, a move designed to lower the barrier of entry for individual retail investors.

AI Security and Strategic Partner Initiatives

  • AIDR Pipeline Growth:
    • CrowdStrike entered Q2 with an AI Detection and Response (AIDR) pipeline of over $50 million, representing 250% quarter-over-quarter growth.
    • The technology helps enterprises deploy AI safely by detecting and neutralizing rogue AI agents.
  • Project Glasswing & OpenAI:
    • Partnering with Anthropic on Project Glasswing to secure AI models.
    • Actively participating in OpenAI’s TAC program, positioning CrowdStrike at the epicenter of AI-driven cybersecurity.
  • Project Quiltworks:
    • A strategic coalition involving top-tier integrators like Accenture and EY.
    • Partners use Falcon technology to conduct AI readiness assessments, identifying millions of exposures in target companies and serving as an acquisition funnel to turn non-customers into active buyers.

The Evolving Threat Landscape and the Falcon Data Moat

  • The Vulnerability Misconception: Kurtz clarified that finding vulnerabilities in source code (which LLMs do well) does not stop breaches. True security requires defense against credential and identity theft, where attackers bypass code completely using stolen tokens.
  • AI Weaponization: AI tools have lowered the entry barrier for cybercriminals, giving rise to more sophisticated, widespread ransomware and extortionware attacks.
  • The Data Advantage: CrowdStrike relies on a proprietary data moat built over 15 years through its Falcon technology to train its defense models far more effectively than newer market entrants.

Market Consolidation and Competitive Strategy

  • Platform Dominance: Kurtz agreed with Cramer that the market is consolidating around a few dominant platform players, heavily favoring comprehensive security suites over point products.
  • Organic Integration vs. M&A:
    • Differentiated CrowdStrike from its primary platform competitor, Palo Alto Networks, by highlighting its ground-up, natively integrated architecture.
    • Positioned CrowdStrike's organic innovation model as superior to competitor strategies reliant on acquiring and stitching together disparate technologies.

Notable Quotes

"Our rule of 40 was 59. So, when you think about the growth and the cash flow generation, it's insane at our scale." — George Kurtz, CEO of CrowdStrike
"You can get rid of all the vulnerabilities in the world and you would still have break-ins because you have... things like identity theft. [Your] credentials, your username, your password, your tokens get stolen, people log into a system and steal all that data." — George Kurtz, CEO of CrowdStrike
"What AI has done is it's created more adversaries with greater sophistication because they're leveraging the models themselves. So, what does that mean? It means more tailwinds for companies like CrowdStrike." — George Kurtz, CEO of CrowdStrike
Last night, CrowdStrike reported what I thought was an excellent quarter. But the stock got hammered today. Mainly because the cybersecurity company didn't beat the estimates by as much as people have become accustomed to. The guidance was strong, too. And they even announced a 4-for-1 stock split, which shouldn't matter in theory, but in practice tends to attract more individual investors. I think this is a buying opportunity. But don't take it from me. Earlier today, I had the chance to speak with George Kurtz, the founder and CEO of CrowdStrike. Take a look. "George, welcome back to Mad Money." "Jim, great to be here." "You know, George, I gotta tell you, I followed your company since you came public, and I saw you had record new annual recurring revenue. You had accelerating revenue growth. You had record cash flow. Your free cash flow was terrific. You had new... new ARR that was great. But somehow, it was regarded as disappointing. So, I have to believe that at some level, you might have been surprised that this quarter was disappointing." "Well, Jim, the way I look at it is we have the best products and we're taking care of our customers in an era where AI is obviously going to be pervasive, and you're going to need security. So, I thought we posted a fantastic quarter. Obviously, you went through some of the stats. The other stat that I'll point out to you is our Rule of 40 was 59. So, when you think about the growth and the cash flow generation, it's insane at our scale. We're very proud of the quarter, and we're excited about the future and the tailwinds that we're seeing from AI." "All right, well, let me, let me posit something. We've had this terrible discovery, basically, this mythos, where it looks like things are way out of control. And I figured that you would have a huge amount of business when this news broke. But it doesn't seem like maybe it turned into a lot of business. Was I wrong?" "Well, Jim, you have to look at the calendar. You're talking about mythos breaking in the middle of April, and our quarter ends at the end of April, right? So, we're selling enterprise software, not necessarily shipping boxes. So, these things take time to get into customers' hands. You've got to go through a sales cycle. But what I want to point out is you have to look at the guidance of the next quarter. We raised the full-year guidance across the entire year, our net new ARR guidance, by over 50 million—over 520 basis points, which is an incredible raise. So, we have the confidence to do that because we see the opportunity that's in front of us. And we see what customers are looking for, which is CrowdStrike—which is our AIDR technology, AI detection and response—to be able to identify rogue AI agents and be able to protect those and allow people to actually accelerate their AI adoption in an enterprise." "Well, I mean, is it possible that people are concerned that you did not raise your near-term ARR? You came in with 5.51 billion versus 5.503 billion, and people thought, maybe the spring rally wasn't justified." "Well, I would point you to the whole year, Jim. You know, 520 basis points is a big move, and we see this taking place across the entirety of the year. So, we feel really good about the raise. I think we feel fantastic about the pipeline. As an example, our AIDR technology, we're entering the pipeline with over 50 million in Q2 alone. We saw 250% quarter-over-quarter growth just in that product alone. And what I know from talking to customers is they want to roll out more AI. And if you need more AI, you want to consume more AI, you're going to need security. So, yeah, I thought it was a great Q1, and we're excited about the future and the guidance that we provide." "All right, well, I couldn't agree more. It's why I told our people who belong to the investing club, 'Don't believe what you read. This is an amazing quarter.' I want to ask about Project Glasswing from Anthropic. What I understand from the people I talk to, you're pretty much leading the cybersecurity efforts to try to figure out what to do here, despite the fact that Anthropic thought at one point that they could handle it themselves. I don't think that insurance companies wanted Anthropic to handle it themselves, and I think they wanted you to do it because I think you actually know how to stop it." "Well, Jim, what's important to realize is that security is an ecosystem. And, to be successful, you need to have an ecosystem that's going to help customers and help partners. So, when you look at what Anthropic has come out with, you know, they've got some great models, no doubt. But in order to be able to work with customers, you have to take advantage of those deep, trusted relationships which companies like CrowdStrike have built over the last 15 years. You have to leverage the deep data moat that we have with our Falcon technology. And you have to leverage the fact that we're in the middle of helping to stop these big breaches, or helping non-customers respond to the breaches they have and then we turn them into customers. So, I think the ecosystem is a key part. They recognize that, and we're happy to be part of that coalition. And, you know, again, we're also working with OpenAI in their TAC program. So, we're at the epicenter of AI and security, and I think that goes to show you the leadership position that we have." "Well, can you talk to me about Project Quiltworks and what you are doing there? Because that seems to be equally as important for the future as what you might be doing in Glasswing." "It is. Well, when you think about Glasswing, when it came out, you had only a handful of companies that have access to mythos. So, you have a lot of customers, their boards, their CEOs, saying, 'What can we do?' I have CEOs calling me in the middle of the night, Fortune 10 companies, going, 'Mythos, you know, help us. What can we do? How does all this work, and how do we make sure we're secure?' So, we're in the middle of these conversations, and the opportunity is so large that you need to create a coalition around this. The Accentures, the EYs of the world, etc., can work using our technology to help customers understand where their exposures are. We're finding millions of exposures in companies that we go into. Those need to be prioritized, and you need to understand what mitigating controls, like Falcon, are in place to be able to prevent against those. So, the great opportunity here is we have our partners drop into either customers or non-customers that we turn into customers because they're using our technology as part of this AI readiness assessment. It's really a multiplier effect for us." "Aren't you surprised that when everything first broke—even in the winter when we realized all the different vulnerabilities—that somehow all of this stuff was believed that it was bad for CrowdStrike? When I know, I know when I first saw it, I said, 'Oh my god, bonanza.' And yet all I read for so long was, 'This is the end of CrowdStrike.' How did people just get it completely backward? What do you think went wrong with that narrative?" "Well, Jim, you have to look at security. There's many different facets. And finding vulnerabilities in source code is one facet of it, okay? And that's what the LLM models do pretty well. But then you have to look at the overall purpose of security: it's to stop the breach. You have to be in line. You have to get it right the first time you see a threat. You've got to be able to create data that allows you to understand what's happening and train that data, and we have a massive proprietary data moat from, you know, over a decade of doing this. So, at the end of the day, we can use these LLMs to be more successful and have greater impact with our customers, but it's only one aspect of what you need to do to build a security platform like Falcon. So, when this came out, I think a lot of people were confused that if you got rid of all the vulnerabilities, the world would be safe. That's not the case. You can get rid of all the vulnerabilities in the world and you would still have break-ins because you have things like identity theft. And what I mean by that is your credentials—your username, your password, your tokens—get stolen, people log into a system, and steal all that data. So, there are many ways to get into a system. Exploiting a vulnerability is just one of those, and you're never going to get rid of all of them. You're still going to need a full lifecycle system, a system of record like Falcon, to be able to stop those breaches." "And look, I know that I've been limiting my questions to this notion of what happens with the Anthropic and mythos. There's been no diminution of the just plain vanilla 'I'm going to wreck your company, pay me' kind of things that CrowdStrike does for a living." "Absolutely not. You know, Jim, we have talked extensively about extortionware and ransomware, right, where these cyber gangs will basically get into a large company or even small companies that don't have the level of protection. It's really shameful. They get in and they extort these folks, and you need security up and down the entire business stack, whether it's a small business or whether it's a large enterprise. And we're only seeing more and more of these sort of attacks. What AI has done is it's created more adversaries with greater sophistication because they're leveraging the models themselves. So, what does that mean? It means more tailwinds for companies like CrowdStrike." "Okay, last question. You know there were so many people in cybersecurity when I first started talking to you. I kind of feel like that it's gotten so big and the threats are so horrible that you need a complete suite. And the only one other than you that has a pretty complete suite would be Nikesh Arora's company, Palo Alto Networks. Is it just you and them? Is it just a duopoly now?" "Well, I think the platform vendors are ultimately going to win. Obviously, it's a big market and, you know, it's a competitive market. But we like where we are given our position, given how we've built the technology from the ground up as being sort of native, integrated technology. And, you know, we come at it just from a different approach of how we drive innovation as opposed to just acquiring a bunch of technologies. But at the end of the day, you have just a few big players that are out there, and I think there's a lot of opportunity for the companies that are out there, though there's only a handful of them, as you pointed out. And I like—I like our chances." "I don't blame you. I'm going to do something that I think someone at least should have done last night: congratulations on a good quarter, George Kurtz, founder and CEO of CrowdStrike. Good to talk to you." "Thanks so much, Jim." "Mad Money will be back after the break."

Dan Loeb: The Lost Art of Short Selling, and Why Stock Picking is Back

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·31:15·69.3K views·2026-06-05
Dan LoebFounder, Third Point
TL;DRDan Loeb, CEO and CIO of Third Point, outlines his evolution from an early internet activist investor to managing a $30 billion multi-strategy platform, emphasizing the return of disciplined short selling, the transition toward quality- and…
AI-driven investing, and his...

TL;DR

Dan Loeb, CEO and CIO of Third Point, outlines his evolution from an early internet activist investor to managing a $30 billion multi-strategy platform, emphasizing the return of disciplined short selling, the transition toward quality- and AI-driven investing, and his successful philanthropic campaign to secure the presidential pardon of Silk Road founder Ross Ulbricht.

The Evolution of Third Point's Investment Strategy

  • Transition from Event-Driven to Business Quality: Third Point's early playbook focused on complex transactions—spin-offs, bankruptcies, and risk arbitrage—where misaligned management incentives allowed co-investors to profit from depressed projections. Today, the strategy has shifted toward business quality, structural moats, and technological adaptability.
      • Subjective Management Assessment: Despite advanced data tools, assessing a management team's ability to stay ahead of disruption remains highly qualitative and relies on pattern recognition built over 30 years.
  • The Multi-Strategy Platform Structure: Under Loeb and co-founder Rob Schwartz, Third Point has diversified away from a single hedge fund model to optimize capital efficiency and risk allocation across several vehicles:
      • Core Long-Short Fund: Focuses on equity long-short and credit (structured credit and high yield).
      • Private Credit & CLOs: Operates direct lending, sponsor financing, and workout solutions.
      • Wholly Owned Insurance Business: Captures investment-grade private and public credit opportunities while utilizing surplus capital dynamically.
  • The Future Role of Humans in Investing: Loeb emphasizes that while AI agents will optimize operations, the human element—networks, relationship-building, and face-to-face qualitative assessment—will remain irreplaceable in managing high-stakes capital allocation.

The Art of Short Selling and Market Dynamics

  • Moving Beyond Purely Valuation-Based Shorts: Loeb cautions against shorting companies purely because they look expensive on paper, noting that Reddit communities and retail trading dynamics can easily trigger devastating short squeezes.
  • The Structured Homebuilder Short: Loeb details a successful short thesis targeting the homebuilding sector:
      • The Thesis: Homebuilders presented themselves as "asset-light" but held massive, hidden capital commitments to expensive land pools.
      • Market Dislocation: A post-COVID hangover of supply chain normalization met unsustainable building costs, leaving buyers unable to afford high home prices in a high-rate, inflationary environment.
  • The Liquidity Dilemma and Distributing Winners: Podcasters Sacks and Palihapitiya joined Loeb in discussing the difficulty of timing exits in public markets. Loeb highlighted past experiences with high-flyers like Palantir, Upstart, and Enphase, noting how leaving corporate boards is often necessary to avoid trading restrictions and preserve liquidity.
  • The Bull Case for Nvidia: Loeb views Nvidia as fundamentally undervalued based on its projected earnings over the next two to three years. He warns that long-short funds seeking symmetry often treat dominant tech giants like Nvidia, Google, or Amazon as "safe shorts," which repeatedly proves to be a costly mistake.

Philanthropy, Education, and Criminal Justice Reform

  • Focusing on Income Inequality Through Education: Loeb argues that addressing income inequality requires equipping vulnerable children with the intellectual tools to compete. He served as the chairman of Success Academies, a charter school network in New York, and advocates for bringing business principles like accountability and meritocracy into the public education sector.
  • The Ross Ulbricht Pardon Campaign: Loeb played a pivotal role in securing the presidential pardon for Silk Road founder Ross Ulbricht, who was serving a double life sentence plus 40 years.
      • The Strategy: Recognizing that the legal system offered no further recourse, Loeb collaborated with activist Charlie Kirk and attorney David Warrington to present the case directly to Donald Trump.
      • The Outcome: Trump commuted and ultimately pardoned Ulbricht, who is now free, married, and starting a family.
  • Individual-Focused Philanthropy: Beyond system-level advocacy, Loeb continues to work on disproportionate sentencing and rehabilitation cases with the Aleph organization, emphasizing that helping individuals "one at a time" is deeply fulfilling.

Notable Quotes

"Activism without proxy contests is like Catholicism without hell." — Dan Loeb
"There's nothing new under the sun. A real focus on management incentives... co-investing with depressed projections and riding along [with] greater transparency." — Dan Loeb
"I've just seen too many people get run over by shorts that have dumb valuations, but they get captured on Reddit or one of these other things." — Dan Loeb
"I think we can all agree that whenever you put the government in charge of something, they'll fuck it up one way or another." — Dan Loeb
Legendary activist investor Dan Loeb. He, of course, is the CEO and CIO of Third Point. The lost art of short selling has come back, and it's absolutely critical. It doesn't matter what you do, you have to be really selective. People talk about a stock picker's market; this is a bond and credit picker's market. When we were small, our main tool was shame and humor. Dan Loeb turned up the heat on Nestle over the weekend. The shift has really been more towards a "dare to be great" message. Activism without proxy contests is like Catholicism without hell. You're very active on Twitter as well; you found your voice. There is a lot of emotion brewing there. Can we actually start with that? Before Twitter, you were actually quite active, but in very different places. I mean, you were in Wall Street Bets before Wall Street Bets existed. Can you just walk us through your evolution as a public persona? Sure. I mean, there was this brand new technology that came out called the internet. Really shortly thereafter, long before Reddit or any of these other things, there were a series of chat boards. There was Yahoo, and there was something called Silicon Investor, among a few other ones. People would congregate and kibbitz. It was done mostly anonymously, and it was an interesting place to exchange ideas. It was really the Wild West; people could pretty much say or do anything. But there was a lot of substance there, too. It's not actually that much different from today. Did you engage at all in any trolling, per se? Well, some people use the term OG. Sometimes I say I was the OT—the original troll. Yeah. No, I did. I mean, it was fun. I didn't know I was one day going to run institutional money and have a big fund; I was just having fun and blowing off steam. Investing is fun, and particularly on the short side. There's so much humor in it when you detect these companies, especially in the '90s. It was really unsupervised, and there were some incredibly fraudulent companies out there. It was just fun to uncover them, kind of taunt the management teams, and ultimately prevail. Do you have one story above others that kind of stands out in that era? I mean, there were a bunch. There was a company called Actrade that I remember, run by a guy who was like a repeat fraudster. We uncovered it, and I know we really got under this person's skin. Ultimately, it was really just a factoring company trading at some large multiple of book value. They had created a new technology called TADS—I don't remember what TADS stood for—but they were basically repackaging factoring securities and saying that they had some special technology. They were financing refrigerators and things like that. Tell us your evolution as an investor. When you started Third Point, you started with very, very little capital. Now it's almost $30 billion of AUM. You're multi-strat. But you learned at Jefferies, I think. You learned helping people like David Tepper allocate capital. Just walk us through how you learned to invest. Well, I started out really fascinated by investing and wanting to do it. I remember when I was 10 years old, my dad was a notoriously bad investor himself, so he didn't give me any good examples. He's a great lawyer, not a great investor. But he took me to meet a broker and I started investing. Then in high school, in the 11th grade, I got a job at the branch office of PaineWebber working for a guy named Alan Crown, who let me post his books and make cold calls. I think we broke certain securities laws, but I think the statute of limitations has passed. I would trade options on Occidental Petroleum and Teledyne. There was a lot of volatility, and I think I had flurries of making money and lost all of it a couple of different times. But it was a good lesson. I continued doing it in college. Then my learning started really formally at Warburg Pincus, where I really learned to value enterprises as my first job, kind of across the spectrum of private equity and venture capital. I worked at a risk arb firm, which was really invaluable. Then, skipping forward, I had way too many jobs in my 20s. But I got really serious at Jefferies, where I had an amazing opportunity to work on the distressed debt desk there. I started out as a research analyst and I was just drinking out of a fire hose. There was so much activity, and the securities were so cheap coming out of distressed. It was the 10,000 hours, 10,000 reps. We would write up different things every day. There were big blocks of debt to move, and that was my real learning point. I stress this to people because everyone kind of sees mentorship as this hierarchical thing where you learn from some wise older person, but I learned a ton from my colleagues, from my own cohort, and I learned a ton from my customers. Eric Mindich was the boy wonder at Goldman—youngest partner at Goldman who ran the arb desk there. He had this triumvirate or quadrumvirate—whatever, the four people. I don't want to leave them out, but Ammos Meron, Dineker, and some other guys. Anyway, they were great, and they really brought me into their thought process thinking about event-driven investing. Then I covered some of the smartest people in the business, including David Tepper. I got to watch their thought process, and I was like a Chinese corporation that was copying and reverse engineering, taking everything in, and creating my database of knowledge and my own operating system. I was kind of taking the best out of what all these different people did. And what was that style when you first started Third Point? What was that expression? Well, I think we called it event-driven investing. It was really less focused on the quality of the business and more focused on very complex transactions—takeovers, spin-offs, risk arbitrage, bankruptcies, privatizations, and demutualizations. These transactions created unbelievable opportunities for Alpha because of the confluence of dislocation, opacity, and time. Nothing changes. I always quote this Jesse Livermore line: "There's nothing new under the sun." There was a real focus on management incentives. In all these different kinds of transactions, management was incentivized to sandbag their numbers during a time when there was an excess supply of securities and where their options were being set. We as co-investors got to come in with these depressed projections and ride along. We got to ride along a few different things that would happen: greater transparency, better understanding of the business, increased coverage, and companies that delivered a top line, margins, and ROE better than expectations. It was really a golden era for that type of investing. And from where that started to what Third Point is today, just describe that. Where do you go from here? Yeah, so stylistically, that event approach is still something we think about; it's in our framework. But I think what happened really when technology became a bigger force—and really everything changed—is a greater focus on business quality, innovation, disruption, and being more thematic. We focus on understanding consumer trends, what's going on in financial services, and what the economic macro backdrop is that's supporting all this. And of course, the big topic of this event, AI, is sort of the culmination of that, along with all of these major technological innovations that have happened since. You could make money before by not being technology savvy in the markets. You could be technologically illiterate or just say, "I don't do it." You could also be, up until the GFC, more or less economically illiterate and still make a lot of money. And now— I wouldn't want to be either one of those things, given how much more important the tech through-line is to understand everywhere. Even if you're like Blue Owl—and Blue Owl obviously is very sophisticated in tech now—any pool of capital that used to not be correlated is effectively correlated. I mean, yeah, you could say that. I just want to answer your question to kind of fast forward and give people a snapshot of what we do today. Rob Schwartz is my partner, and we took Kempo Karate together when we were 10 years old. He was a purple belt, and I think I never made it past a yellow belt. But we reconnected at our 20-year reunion in 1999—I'm aging both of us, sorry to give up your secret, Rob. He was working as a sales rep for wireless RF components. I said, "Wow, this guy would be great to do channel checks for us." Then I asked him a couple of years later, "If you ever come across a really savvy engineer, we should invest." We didn't know what we were doing, and we weren't venture capitalists, but we were getting behind a person. There was a guy named Dave Fisher who started a company called Radia Communications. He made chips that were, I still remember, A-B-G compatible for Wi-Fi base stations, and ultimately the company was sold to Texas Instruments. We've—I won't go deep into our venture business—but we started to do that within the fund. We've done a couple of dedicated funds, so we have that strand of activity. We can talk a little bit more about what we're thinking and how we're seeing this. Ultimately, what I want to get to is that all these things are interconnected and come together under the platform we have today. We have the main hedge fund which does credit and equity long-short. The credit side is both structured credit and high yield. We also have a CLO business that we acquired. We started a private credit business that does traditional private credit, direct sponsor financing, direct lending, and workouts—which is very important—or "credit solutions," as they call it. There is a lot to do there. Then we started an insurance company a few years ago. It's not the first insurance company we've done—we did a P&C company before—but this one was wholly owned, and now we own half of it. The insurance company captures basically the investment-grade part of what we do: private credit through structured vehicles, structured credit, whole loans, and investment-grade debt, both private and public. We can also use our surplus capital in very interesting ways. So what's the role of the human, and what's the role of Dan Loeb in running Third Point ten years from now? Ten years ago, Dan Loeb was 100% of Third Point. Now there are agents, there is AI, there is all this learning, and all of this data. Where do you see the role of the human, and where do you see the role of systems making decisions, allocating capital, and managing risk? I mean, first of all, my time is spent primarily on managing the hedge fund, which for now is the biggest capital pool and the most important business that we're in. The human element—and I think this is true for everyone you have here—the social component, the human network of knowing people, being able to capture opportunities, work with people, and interact is never going away. Maybe you can theorize that there will be agents sitting at Andreessen Horowitz or your funds, but I think the human will always have to be there because people like to— They want to know who's making or losing the money. Yeah. And I think agents or AI will never really be able to look you in the eye and assess all of those things. You've expanded your philosophy of investing in companies from cheap securities with catalysts, as I think you described it on a podcast recently, and now you're very concerned about moats, defensibility, and the quality or "brittleness," as Chamath likes to remind us, of the revenue. Could you tell us how you evolved that core thinking about the quality of companies, and maybe give us some examples of companies that now fit through that filter where you feel they have a moat and durability? Yeah, obviously that's everything right now. Chamath talks about the time-bounded value of companies, and I think that's essential. What are the companies that are going to be around seven, ten, or twenty years from now? What are the real moats that exist out there? It is harder now. I think we deluded ourselves earlier. If you asked people about the moat around IBM, AOL, or Yahoo back then, they would say the same things. Today, we're investing outside of tech into companies that have great qualities, but it also comes back to the management. We can't really just look at a product or a technology and say, "Oh, this is going to be it forever." We really look for a management team that we think will be adaptable. Just like you guys were saying last night, you don't want to be on boards of companies. These are things management should be doing. I think finding management teams that you really believe in, who have a proven ability to stay ahead, is a huge part of it. Is that quantifiable, or is it still very much a subjective, qualitative— Sorry, is what? Is assessing the management team quantifiable? Have you built a rubric for doing that? No, it's still very subjective and qualitative. I think it's one of those things where, after thirty years, there is a pattern recognition and you— Let me ask a question on screening. You've recently said publicly that there are a lot of opportunities on the short side in the market right now, for the first time in a long time. How do you start? Is it a top-down or is it opportunistic? Does something come across the wire and you guys jump on it in an event-driven way, or do you have a systematic, top-down approach to looking at the market and finding those opportunities? Yeah, there's no one approach to it. I think one thing we've avoided is a solely valuation-based approach. I've seen too many people get run over by shorts that have dumb valuations, but they get captured on Reddit or one of those other things and they just get destroyed. Look at some of these space companies right now—there's no rhyme or reason. We had a really strong view on home builders from last year that there were two things going on. It wasn't just interest rates and mortgage spreads depressing housing prices. The home building industry was structurally impaired because of the way they were all pretending to be NVR, which is to say pretending to be asset-light. But they actually had massive commitments to these land pools, in vehicles they said were options but they were actually very committed to in terms of capital. The home building industry was really the last industry that had this post-COVID hangover of inventory disruptions and pricing that made no sense. Prices went up to unsustainable levels, but so did building costs, and buyers are no longer able to pay those prices in the current financing environment. They've also been squeezed by inflation and costs, so we've been shorting things related to that. Let me bring Sacks into the discussion here. Sacks, we've learned a little bit about the distribution of public securities. Your famous All-In theme song has this great quote: "Let your winners ride." I'm curious, when you hear Dan talking about this, how you think as a private market investor about navigating distributing equities, and how you've sharpened your blade regarding which ones have brittle or more robust revenue? I'm sure you guys share this; it's one of the most vexing questions. We were private investors in Palantir, and I think we sold all of our stock in the 20s. Gosh. Missed a 10x after going public, or an 8x or something. We were private and we led the B-round in Upstart. That was one where I think we learned not to go on boards anymore, because it restricts your ability to be liquid. We were also early investors in Enphase, and we sold some stock on the IPO, took a tax hit, and then I think sold it under a dollar. Had we stayed on, that stock would have made $4 billion. So I am not claiming to have any great expertise in knowing how to best distribute our— Dude, markets are brutal. It's so hard. I mean, no, this is why I bring it up. We've all struggled with this. Sacks, where have you wound up? I think it's case-by-case. There are some companies where—like when I was on a board, you can't sell and you end up regretting that. Then there are others where the best thing to do is just hold on to that stock forever. Examples in your portfolio where you made great decisions? Well, I'm not going to talk about the ones that didn't do so well. But no, I mean, look, I've owned Meta and Palantir as a private, venture, and angel investor. And you sold? Well, I sold some and held on to some. Obviously, in hindsight, you take Meta—I think Meta IPOed, when it was Facebook back then, at a $50 billion valuation. Actually, $102 billion. Yeah, but it went down to $18 billion. Yeah. Can you imagine in an alternate universe, if Chamath never sold his Facebook, how insufferable he'd be? Or if Friedberg never sold his Google, Friedberg would be worth $10 billion. No, I wouldn't be nearly as good. What's that? I wouldn't be nearly as good. Like an analyst? Because you created—I mean, it would have been nice, but it's not real, it's not earned. Back in those days ten years ago, we thought a $100 billion market cap company was pretty much as big as anything could get. Facebook at 50 or whatever, it was like the upside was to 100. Things are just totally different now. We have multi-trillion dollar companies, the market is so much bigger, and that changes— I mean, that's the rub against Nvidia, which is a $5 trillion company. People feel like there's sort of a ceiling on it. I think we'll look back at some point in time and say that was a foolish way to think about Nvidia, given its dominant position and its valuation relative to everyone else. Is it undervalued right now? Yeah, absolutely, on earnings over the next two or three years. Is it because people are having a hard time processing the largest entity that has ever existed in human history? I think that, and the narrative that—well, first of all, technically there's all this other stuff that's growing faster and going up more. People are—and the long-short pods are structured such that they have to be short something, so Nvidia feels like a safe short. By the way, Google was a safe short, and Amazon was a safe short. This just happens. Sometimes they languish at a valuation, and then they break out. I think that will eventually happen with Nvidia. But there's probably some boundary condition discount to that, right? We've never seen a valuation like this. You can't overbet that. I want to shift topics for a second. I just want to talk society and culture before we run out of time with you. There was this incredible thing that you told me, which I relate to these guys: you're very passionate about criminal justice reform, and specifically, you were a key person in getting the pardon of Ross Ulbricht. Tell us your views on criminal justice, why it hit such a nerve, and then why Ross Ulbricht? What happened there that made you say, "I must fight for this guy"? Well, let me take a step back and talk about my framework for philanthropy. I think, not unlike Brad Gerstner and many people in the room here—and I would say everybody up here—I care deeply about income inequality. I care deeply about making sure that as many people as possible have opportunities to experience the incredible things that we've all had here. My interest in criminal justice reform really started earlier, with an interest in education and education reform. I was very lucky to start supporting, get on the board of, and ultimately be chairman of Success Academies, which is a charter school network in New York. Nobody talks about it, but the thing hiding in plain sight for everyone is that the problem with income inequality isn't that Jeff Bezos is going to be a trillionaire or that all these other people are gaining wealth. It's that we're not equipping children, and particularly the most vulnerable children, with the intellectual tools they need to succeed and compete. It's not because poverty is this intractable thing that can't be overcome; we've proven that it can be. The problem is that the unions and the basic principles we all use in business—which are accountability, merit, and cultivating talent—are set aside for the benefit of adults who are part of these unions. It's a systemic thing. It's not a lack of money; it's really a broken structure. Accountability is what I'm hearing. Yeah. So I spent a lot of time on that; we can leave it at that. I then became aware—and it was interesting, I was looking for issues that conservatives and liberals, or progressives, can agree on. It was great to see Fetterman and McCormick up here. Hopefully, we can agree that we want young people to be better educated. We can also agree that whenever you put the government in charge of something, they will mess it up one way or another. I want to give you guys a shout-out, though, for not messing up this private-public partnership with investments in the private sector. I think this administration has done an enormously good job of backing companies, but let's put that aside. That's one of the rare instances where I've seen that. Can you give an example of that, standing out in your mind? We have a company in our portfolio called Atom Computing that, along with many other quantum companies, has gotten money from the government. We were super impressed by how they contracted with us to engage in cryptography and meet the government's needs, but also in the financial component. They drove a really tough bargain. The government and taxpayers are going to make a ton of money on this, and their involvement will contribute meaningfully to the value of this business. It's just a win all the way around. So they're an investor and a customer. Right. And they are capturing part of that value as a customer for the American people, which I think everybody deserves. Okay, so back to criminal justice reform. First of all, there are a lot of bad people in jail. I think the criminal justice movement has been undermined by folks who see it as an opportunity to not prosecute or deal with the bad people out there. But there are also a lot of people who are rehabilitated. There are really three different categories: people who are falsely convicted, people who have shown contrition and rehabilitation, and those who had a disproportionate sentence relative to what they did. There's a case right now of a guy named Jonathan Grobman, who dealt in gray-market diapers and formula and got an 18-year sentence for dealing those goods. In the case of Ross Ulbricht, I was approached by someone, and this just seemed like too much. Ross, as people in this room probably know, was sort of a folk hero because of his cat-and-mouse game with the government. He ran Silk Road, one of the first crypto-based exchanges. He acknowledges that he did things that were illegal and that he shouldn't have done; he regrets it. Drugs and other things were dealt on the exchange, which is what he was accused of. The government later said there were murder-for-hire incidents, but he was never prosecuted for that and he denies it ever happened. In any case, he was sentenced to double life plus 40 years. Who knows how he got the extra 40 years or how he would spend them after two lifetimes. A woman I met named Riva Teas alerted me to this, who was friends with Olaf Carlson-Wee and other crypto insiders. I thought about it and said, "This guy has no way out. There is no recourse through the system to get someone with a life sentence out of jail; this will only work with a presidential pardon." We worked on it, having some familiarity with the pardon process. Then I approached Charlie Kirk about it. Charlie really embraced this and saw Ross as someone who had been unfairly sentenced. He took it to the president. Charlie also had an attorney named David Warrington, who is currently the White House counsel. I just found out a couple of days ago that he had been his lawyer for a decade. I'm not taking credit for this, and I'm not saying Charlie does either—it takes a village. David had been working on it, and on the last day of Trump's term, we were certain he was going to get out. But the Justice Department, for whatever reason, told the president, "If you commute his sentence, we're going to go after you." As I understand it, the president withdrew the commutation. Four years went by, and Charlie really took the lead on this—this was his only ask of the president. The president promised libertarians and the crypto community to deal with this. Not only was his sentence commuted, but he was pardoned. Today, Ross is married, is having a child, and is living a free life after spending a decade in prison. And do you feel like you should—is there a role for you to play in doing more of this? Was this a one-off, or—? Yeah, no, I continue to work on cases. There's an organization called Aleph, and we work constantly on different people. I feel like, as philanthropists, it's great to work with organizations, and there are a lot of great organizations I work with. I also do a lot of work fighting antisemitism and supporting Jewish identity. But I also think that we can help people one at a time. It really nurtures the soul, and I think it's just a good thing to do. Amazing. All right, let's give it up for Dan. Dan Loeb. Thank you to our partners: NYSE, EY, Athena, Polymarket, S-AI, Ondo, Forge, Plaud, LH, Manhattan, DeLonghi, Accelevents, SecondLane, Velocity Black, Stillcore Capital, Caldera Lab, and Niagen.

Databricks CEO on Unifying Data & AI Agents

Bloomberg Live(187.0K)·17:37·2.2K views·2026-06-05
Ali GhodsiCo-Founder & CEO, Databricks Tom GilesReporter, Bloomberg
TL;DRFollowing a $5 billion funding round at a $134 billion valuation, Databricks is experiencing unprecedented, agent-driven growth and is expanding into the cybersecurity market with its new product, Lakewatch.
CEO Ali Ghodsi views 2026 as a highly unfavorable year for an IPO due...

TL;DR

Following a $5 billion funding round at a $134 billion valuation, Databricks is experiencing unprecedented, agent-driven growth and is expanding into the cybersecurity market with its new product, Lakewatch. CEO Ali Ghodsi views 2026 as a highly unfavorable year for an IPO due to macroeconomic instability, choosing instead to remain private and potentially raise more capital as agentic AI and open-source models reshape enterprise tech.

Capital Allocation and Business Acceleration

  • Massive Capital Infusion: Databricks recently raised $5 billion in equity at a $134 billion valuation to support rapid business acceleration across all global regions (US, Europe, Latin America, and Asia) and cloud environments.
  • Investment Priorities:
      • Core Infrastructure: Expanding the core data platform, security features, and the Lakehouse database.
      • Conversational AI: Accelerating development of Genie, Databricks' conversational AI tool that allows users to query data using natural language.
      • AI Research & Compute: Hiring hundreds of specialized AI researchers and investing heavily in GPUs to support advanced reinforcement learning research.
  • The Agentic Paradigm Shift:
      • Surpassing Human Activity: For the first time, AI agents are generating more database traffic on Databricks than humans. Over 50% of total queries on the platform are now generated by agents.
      • Automated Database Launches: Over 81% of database launches on the Databricks platform are now initiated by automated AI agents, driving consumption-based revenues rapidly.

The Security Imperative: Entering the Market with Lakewatch

  • Disrupting the Security Market: Databricks is launching "Lakewatch," a security product designed to protect enterprises from automated AI threats.
      • The "SaaS-pocalypse" Effect: AI has lowered software development barriers, compressing the time required for Databricks to build and launch competitive security software by 10x.
      • AI-Driven Threats: Threat actors are increasingly automated. Ghodsi cited "X-BOW" (an automated agent) as a top threat actor, alongside instances of models like OpenAI’s o3 being used to hack the Linux kernel. Legacy security providers are fundamentally ill-equipped to handle this volume of agentic attacks.
  • Organizational Strategy:
      • Small, Nimble Teams: Databricks is staffing its security division in the hundreds rather than thousands.
      • Avoiding "Coordination Overhead": In the AI era, Ghodsi argues that massive teams slow down execution due to excessive coordination requirements.

AI Infrastructure Trends: Shrinking LLM Lifespans and Rising Costs

  • One-Month Model Cycles: The dominance window for any single frontier Large Language Model (LLM) has shrunk from six months to just one month, as rapid iterations from providers keep developers constantly switching to the newest, cheapest, or smartest option.
  • Enterprise Cost Controls: To combat soaring AI costs, enterprises are adopting the "advisor model" utilizing Databricks' Unity AI Gateway:
      • Hybrid Architecture: Companies use a cheap, fast, open-source model for basic tasks and grant it tool access to call a larger, expensive model (like Claude or Gemini) only when it gets stuck.
      • Budget Stabilization: This strategy has successfully flattened enterprise AI cost curves even as total usage continues to climb.
  • The Rise of Chinese Open-Source Models: Despite geopolitical friction, Chinese open-source models (such as Kimi) are highly disruptive globally due to massive price discrepancies. Ghodsi noted that many enterprises choose these models when faced with a choice between a $1 million Chinese open-source alternative versus a $30 million US counterpart.

IPO Postponement and the Future of Private Capital

  • 2026 is a "Terrible" Year for IPOs: Ghodsi reaffirmed his stance against a near-term IPO, citing market volatility from elections, macroeconomic indicators, energy uncertainties, and the massive, unprecedented capital demands of upcoming mega-IPOs.
  • Private Market Resilience: Databricks has no immediate pressure to go public and may raise another private round.
  • Capital Liquidity: As other giant private firms eventually transition to public markets, Ghodsi expects a high supply of remaining private capital to seek late-stage companies like Databricks and Stripe.
