Delivering AI to Every Farmer in America | USDA at AIPCon 10

Palantir(153.0K)·15:56·1.7K views·2026-06-05
Sam BerryChief Information Officer, USDA
TL;DRThe U.S.
Department of Agriculture (USDA) has modernized its fragmented, $1 billion-per-year legacy IT infrastructure using Palantir to establish the "One Farmer, One File" ontology. This secure, AI-ready data foundation has streamlined acreage reporting and facilitated the...

TL;DR

The U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA) has modernized its fragmented, $1 billion-per-year legacy IT infrastructure using Palantir to establish the "One Farmer, One File" ontology. This secure, AI-ready data foundation has streamlined acreage reporting and facilitated the fastest capital injection in USDA history, distributing $4.4 billion to farmers in just five days.

Overcoming Legacy System Fragmentation and Failed Modernization

  • The Legacy Tech Burden: Prior to the current administration, the USDA's farmer-facing IT architecture cost nearly $1 billion taxpayer dollars annually to maintain. It was supported by thousands of contractors managing hundreds of fragmented legacy systems that were highly vulnerable to cyber threats and unable to communicate with one another.
  • History of Failed Modernization: A previous 10-year modernization effort ran hundreds of millions of dollars over budget. While a congressional audit estimated only 15% of the promised deliverables were actually completed, internal USDA staff estimated the true figure was closer to 5%.
  • The Shift to Ontology: Rather than building more pipelines, the USDA partnered with Palantir to establish a centralized semantic framework. Known internally as "One Farmer, One File," this ontology acts as a single, governed source of truth that translates disparate data into clear agricultural concepts (farmers, farms, and payments).

The Landmark Platform and Record-Breaking Capital Deployment

  • The Landmark Platform: Operating on top of the "One Farmer, One File" ontology, Landmark provides digital-first tools that eliminate administrative red tape, allowing farmers to report acreage and certify payments from home.
  • GEAR (Acreage Reporting Tool): Currently being deployed to county offices, GEAR enables USDA staff and farmers to easily subdivide, report, and certify land acreage in one virtual location, saving critical daylight hours for agricultural workers.
  • The Farmer Bridge Assistance Program:
      • Program Scale: An $11 billion program designed to keep row-crop producers solvent during market volatility.
      • Unprecedented Speed: Upon opening, the program broke all prior USDA records for online signups within 62 minutes, deploying over $4.4 billion directly to farmers within its first five days.
      • Orchestration and Accountability: Landmark integrated automated background workflows with intentional human review checkpoints (e.g., state office reviews), ensuring that all financial decisions remain auditable, logged, and secure.

Next-Generation AI and National Security Protection

  • Landmark Vision: The USDA is piloting next-generation vision models that allow farmers to upload photos of reporting documents. The AI automatically extracts crop types, planting dates, irrigation statuses, and field boundaries directly into the ontology to trigger downstream workflows without manual data entry.
  • Governed AI Integration: By pointing Large Language Models (LLMs) at a governed ontology with built-in permissions and data lineage, the USDA avoids the hallucination, security, and auditability risks associated with querying disconnected legacy databases.
  • Admin Command Center: This centralized visibility platform allows the USDA to proactively identify supply chain risks:
      • Foreign Land Ownership: Digitizes and tracks foreign investment in American farmland to monitor national security threats.
      • Environmental Resilience: Generates comprehensive predictive modeling on the impact of droughts and other adverse weather events on the national food supply.

A Call to Action for Federal Technology Procurement

  • Eliminate Cost-Overrun Culture: Federal IT contractors and integrators must stop viewing government projects as permanent opportunities for high-cost, low-yield modernization projects.
  • Embrace Lean Engineering: Government agencies and private sector technology partners must prioritize agile, outcome-focused cultures, using foundationally AI-enabled platforms to deliver secure solutions on highly aggressive timelines.

Notable Quotes

"Everyone wants to implement AI and deliver stronger, more powerful solutions, but we know that pointing an LLM at hundreds of disconnected ungoverned databases gets you a system that hallucinates, is insecure, and is unauditable. For something as consequential as our nation's agricultural data, that is not just useless, it's dangerous." — Sam Berry
"Within 62 minutes of opening, the program broke all prior USDA records for online farmer signups... It delivered over $4.4 billion directly to farmers in the program's first five days." — Sam Berry
"Federal technology providers and their affiliates, their integrators and their contractors must stop treating the government as a permanent opportunity for cost-overrun modernization efforts that too often lead to nowhere. Instead, pursue federal business with foundationally AI-enabled platforms, with lean engineering teams, with rapid and aggressive deployment timelines." — Sam Berry
Please welcome Chief Information Officer of the US Department of Agriculture, Sam Berry. Hi, I'm Sam Berry, Chief Information Officer at the most important civilian department in the federal government, the Department of Agriculture. Going all the way back to the creation of our country, the Founding Fathers were passionate about the criticality of agriculture in national sovereignty, security, and even morality. President George Washington famously said, "It will not be doubted that with reference to either individual or national welfare, agriculture is of primary importance." To this day, agriculture remains a foundational element to our nation's success, both domestically and geopolitically. Look no further than the current conflict in the Middle East and its disruption to the fertilizer supply chain. Countries that are minimally self-sufficient are left in a state of emergency, while the United States is able to adapt and persevere. This self-sufficiency is not by accident, and it requires our constant attention to defend and protect as a national security asset. In that effort, it is important for us to realize that our most consequential battlefield is invisible. It resides in the networks, machinery, and data that power the core infrastructure of our nation, including the agriculture industry. Prior to this administration, the technology behind USDA's most important systems was stuck in a different era, with hundreds of fragmented legacy systems, none of them designed to talk to each other. Here's how that translates to our farmer-facing systems. What you are looking at is an architecture that costs nearly a billion taxpayer dollars a year to support. It involves thousands of contractors, hundreds of systems, and a cybersecurity challenge, to put it mildly. An environment like this makes it impossible to deliver a positive experience to our citizens. Fragments of farmer and farm records are duplicated and distributed across dozens of systems with little to no communication. USDA previously attempted to transform this, and it resulted in a 10-year modernization project that ran hundreds of millions of dollars over budget. One audit reported to Congress that an estimated 15% of what was promised actually got delivered. The people inside USDA will tell you that that number is actually much closer to 5%. So, hundreds of millions of dollars across multiple administrations resulted in essentially zero progress. That is the estate that our current administration inherited. Thankfully, we have a team with private sector experience who is willing to focus purely on outcomes to rebuild our systems with speed and precision using modern, state-of-the-art technology. We started at the foundation because all of our problems were rooted in the fact that data was everywhere. This wasn't just a case of building pipelines. We had to start at the meta-level and define the semantics for our department. For example, what are the different attributes, documents, and historical information that define a farmer? What are the congressionally required details and programs? Then, we did the same exercise for a farm, and then the same exercise for a payment. These are all actually much more complex than you would expect. We realized that we were faced with an impossible task, and that the only way we could deliver on our mandate to reduce cost and improve security would be to take an entirely new approach. That is what led us to Palantir. With Palantir, we centralized USDA's core data assets under a single unified model of reality. One record, one source of truth for each farmer. That's Palantir's ontology, and at the USDA, we call it "one farmer, one file." It's the difference between data that sits in systems and context that actually understands the mission it serves. And this, of course, was key to delivering the latest technology. Everyone wants to implement AI and deliver stronger, more powerful solutions, but we know that pointing an LLM at hundreds of disconnected, ungoverned databases gets you a system that hallucinates, is insecure, and is unauditable. For something as consequential as our nation's agricultural data, that is not just useless, it's dangerous. The ontology has been the key to delivering AI-enabled technology to every farmer in the country. The LLMs will now be able to wield this governed model of reality—real farmers, real acreage, real programs—with permissions and lineage built in. On that foundation we built Landmark. Landmark is the platform powering "one farmer, one file." It cuts through the red tape farmers face when they need something from USDA. It gives them and our staff digital-first tools they can use from home, reducing the time to complete tasks such as acreage reporting and payment certification, improving our overall ability to support the farmer without complicating the process. Here's the principle behind it: we're bringing this technology directly to the Americans who grow this country's food, people who would otherwise have little or no access to such tools. For a farmer, every hour of daylight matters. They shouldn't have to leave their land to receive critical services. Acreage reporting was a natural first area of focus, as it is something that nearly every farmer interfaces with. It is also the foundational data component to our larger national picture. This enables us to better understand our agricultural position so that we can respond to adverse events, provide intelligent recommendations to farmers on what crops will yield most successfully, and develop tooling which will detect foreign ownership of our farmlands. All of this while delivering a much better experience to farmers and saving them hours or days of time. We are currently deploying GEAR, the tool that you see behind me, to county offices across the country to support USDA staff in reporting acreage in partnership with farmers. This streamlines the process, allowing users to subdivide land and report and certify acreage in one spot. For the first time ever, farmers will soon be able to report their own acreage independently or work with county staff to do it on their behalf. So, we started with the operational side of agriculture, meeting farmers where they needed us today. But concurrently, we were able to quickly build on that same ontology to deliver major new initiatives like the Farmer Bridge Assistance Program. For the first time ever, farmers could certify their information from the comfort of their home with a single click. One farmer told us that he submitted his application on his phone while boarding an airplane. This was the fastest deployment of capital to farmers in the history of this country. It was an $11 billion program that provided payments to row-crop producers to stay solvent during market volatility. The program offered immediate cash flow relief to secure operations for the planting season while long-term, market-driven policy improvements are implemented. Within 62 minutes of opening, the program broke all prior USDA records for online farmer signups. Farmers were able to enroll without visiting a county office. It delivered over $4.4 billion directly to farmers in the program's first five days. This wasn't a case of just building a new front-end portal for farmers to apply. Instead, this utilized all of the connectivity of the ontology, enabling us to orchestrate the delivery of this program across multiple stages, their respective teams, and processes. Here, we see one of those applications moving through the whole process. Palantir enabled us to connect these stages end-to-end, with human operators and technology working in parallel to ensure critical reviews were completed efficiently and accurately. Some steps are fully automated, taking care of the repetitive, mechanical work that used to take days. Others, like state office review, are intentionally done by a person. The human review isn't a bottleneck we failed to remove; it's a checkpoint that we chose to keep. When you're deciding whether a farmer gets paid, someone must be accountable for that decision. Every one of those decisions is logged and auditable. The platform allows us to control automation and apply it where it is needed. The result is that applications don't sit waiting. Actions, reviews, and updates are timely, and our farmers get what they need faster than ever before. Then, of course, this has to scale. Thousands of farmers are flowing through the process, all orchestrated on Landmark. Auditability is critical here. Every action taken against an application is logged and can be audited if needed. We're running this process with better visibility and accuracy than ever before, without having to compromise on speed. So, you've seen how we were able to rapidly solve longstanding challenges for our department and deliver transformational programs to every farmer in the country. Now, I want to talk about what's next and how solving these challenges unlocks USDA to start thinking about delivering truly next-generation tooling to every American farmer. Jumping back to acreage reporting, Landmark has already reduced the time it takes for county offices to report acreage and support our most critical programs. Now, we're looking at how we can make this even more seamless with AI. Landmark Vision represents the next era, where farmers simply capture an image of their reporting documents and vision models extract all the details from the report. The crop type, plant date, irrigation status, and fields can all be captured using models, making it easier for farmers and staff to input information. Critically, this is built within our existing ontology context. This ensures the models are deployed with the appropriate guardrails and evaluations to ensure incorrect or infeasible information isn't captured. That connected ontology means that as soon as the details are extracted, all downstream actions and workflows are automatically kicked off, minimizing the need for the farmer to do any manual data entry. Workflows like this are positioning USDA for the future, and this is only the start. Let's take a step back and think about the system as a whole. A platform like this only works if the people it serves trust it. How farmers' data is used and protected is of paramount importance to us, and it is a significant part of why we're working with Palantir. Building everything on a single governed model means security isn't a wall we put around hundreds of fragmented systems. Instead, it's built into every aspect of the ontology. The same permission model that keeps our AI workflows evaluated and accurate is what protects the farmer. Every workflow, every integration, and every piece of farmer data is more secure than it has ever been, and every action can be traced. That same approach extends to a much larger national security mandate. The same ontology powers what we call the Admin Command Center—centralized visibility across the department to recognize risk before it reaches the food supply. Take foreign investment in American farmland. Tracking who owns and who is buying US agricultural land used to be a slow, manual process. We're digitizing it and giving USDA clear visibility into land ownership so risk is identified quickly and proactively. Here, we get a full view of the US agricultural landscape while also having visibility into threats and scenarios that could harm farmers and their ability to provide food. For example, we are now able to produce comprehensive reporting on the impact of a potential drought. These signals equip USDA with the data we need to ensure our food supply remains resilient in every scenario. You see what is possible with this incredible technology, the pace that we can move when efficiency and outcomes are the focus, how operations can be transformed, and how the entire posture of our nation can shift through AI and proper data orchestration. But this is only the start. We still have a lot of work to do inside of USDA and beyond. Zooming out to the state of our republic, we have an imbalance in what we are capable of doing and what we are actually doing. In many ways, decadence and abundance have led to excessive corruption and waste. We have forty-plus million Americans dependent on state-sponsored food programs. Large corporations are poisoning our food supply in favor of profits. Politicians are pushing our country towards global dependence while allowing foreign adversaries to buy up our land. Intellectualism and technology are distracting our society and removing us from what it truly means to be human—to be one with the land and to be one with how we nourish our families, communities, and nation. We have work to do. We need builders and innovators to activate and become excited about partnering with the government and serving our country. We need technology companies to operate as if every decision is life or death, leading to motivated, vibrant, hardworking cultures that develop products like what you see coming out of Palantir. Federal technology providers and their affiliates, their integrators, and their contractors must stop treating the government as a permanent opportunity for cost-overrun modernization efforts that too often lead to nowhere. Instead, they must pursue federal business with foundationally AI-enabled platforms, with lean engineering teams, and with rapid and aggressive deployment timelines. Learn from Palantir, and our country will be stronger, more secure, and more capable because of it. As an American, right now is the greatest time to be alive. We have the unique opportunity of being on the cusp of a golden age. All that we have to do is make it happen. We are on the frontier of AI. We have a president, a cabinet, and a general rising political class that is relentlessly pursuing American dominance and independence. We have a younger generation that is hungry, innovative, and ready to build like we've never built before. Data fusion and AI make every single person more capable. USDA is equipping farmers with the advanced technology they deserve, improving the resiliency of their operations while keeping our great nation secure. Thank you.

TL;DRNVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang and Dell Technologies CEO Michael Dell unveiled the next generation of the Dell AI Factory powered by NVIDIA, highlighting the transition to autonomous "agentic" AI systems that run across a unified architecture scaling from…

TL;DR

NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang and Dell Technologies CEO Michael Dell unveiled the next generation of the Dell AI Factory powered by NVIDIA, highlighting the transition to autonomous "agentic" AI systems that run across a unified architecture scaling from local desktop workstations to massive enterprise racks.

The Rise of Agentic AI and Parabolic Compute Demand

  • The Evolution of AI: Generative AI has transitioned from simply generating content to generating "thoughts" (reasoning, planning, and autonomous execution).
      • This shift has led to the rise of agentic systems that run autonomously, use digital tools, analyze results, and iteratively refine plans to complete tasks.
  • Explosive Computational Needs: Because autonomous agents iterate over long periods rather than responding to single queries, computational demand has scaled by 100x to 1,000x.
  • Enterprise-Scale Adoption: Mainstream enterprises (including Eli Lilly, Samsung, and Honeywell) are deploying agent networks across critical workflows.
      • Workflow Impact: Agentic software is currently managing high-value engineering tasks including software development, DevOps, SRE, QA testing, and CI/CD pipelines.
      • The New Engineering Paradigm: Individual software engineers will transition from writing code directly to orchestrating vast tiers of primary agents and sub-agents.

Next-Generation Dell and NVIDIA Hardware Portfolio

  • Unified Scale-Up Architecture: Dell and NVIDIA's new hardware line relies on a single, consistent architecture that scales from desktop workstations to enterprise data center racks.
  • The NVLink 72 System: Serving as the massive central "brain," this scale-up single domain computer can house frontier large language models (LLMs) ranging from 1 to 10 terabytes of parameters.
  • Workstation Desktop ("The Station"): Powered by the GB300, this desktop setup can run a 1-trillion parameter model locally—a computing feat that previously required a full cloud data center.
  • The GB10 Workstation: Representing the smallest entry in this hardware lineup, the GB10 brings the exact same architecture down to an accessible, compact footprint.

Grace CPU and the Economics of Token Generation

  • A Paradigm Shift in Economics: In the AI era, performance is measured by token generation speed rather than the traditional cloud hosting model of renting CPU cores.
  • Grace CPU Capabilities: Specifically designed for agentic processing, the Grace CPU features:
      • The highest single-threaded performance of any CPU in the world.
      • Three times the memory bandwidth of the fastest traditional CPUs.
  • Eliminating Database Bottlenecks: Highly active agents constantly query databases. The high memory bandwidth of the Grace CPU ensures that databases like Starburst and DuckDB run fast enough to prevent idling the massive GPU arrays waiting on them.

Hybrid Deployment, Security, and "Unmetered Intelligence"

  • Hybrid AI Ecosystem: The shared architecture supports every frontier model (including deep integration with Anthropic) and open-source models, whether run locally, in private data centers, or across hyperscale public clouds.
  • Confidential Computing: Built-in hardware-level confidential computing protects sensitive enterprise data, ensuring companies do not have to trust third-party cloud data center operators.
  • Eliminating "Token Anxiety": By running architectures locally on private Dell infrastructure, enterprises can deploy "unmetered intelligence" using proprietary data without recurring per-token cloud billing or usage caps.

Notable Quotes

"A really great engineer in the future is going to be orchestrating a whole bunch of agents who are going to be orchestrating a whole bunch of sub-agents to do work." — Jensen Huang, CEO of NVIDIA
"You’re not renting CPU cores anymore, you’re generating tokens... That’s the economics of this AI era." — Jensen Huang, CEO of NVIDIA
"I love this idea of the unmetered intelligence, where you’ve got the power in your own PC, in your own data center and you can use it with your own data... You don't have to struggle with token anxiety!" — Michael Dell, CEO of Dell Technologies

Donald Trump LIVE: Trump Drops BOMBSHELL on India-US Trade Deal | PM Modi | Trump Tariffs |Trade War

India Today(11.2M)·252:51·13.5K views·2026-06-05

TL;DRPresident Donald Trump announced a massive $700 million federal revitalization of the U.S.
coal sector—including invoking the Defense Production Act to save 13 coal plants—while declaring that a major bilateral trade deal with India is "around the corner" despite...

TL;DR

President Donald Trump announced a massive $700 million federal revitalization of the U.S. coal sector—including invoking the Defense Production Act to save 13 coal plants—while declaring that a major bilateral trade deal with India is "around the corner" despite historical tariff imbalances.

## Revitalizing Coal and Securing U.S. Energy Dominance

  • DPA Invocation and Plant Protection: The administration officially invoked the Defense Production Act (DPA) to keep 13 coal plants operating across 10 states, including West Virginia, Kentucky, North Carolina, and Wisconsin.
  • Federal Funding and Private Match: Announced a $700 million federal investment—complemented by $1.7 billion in private capital—to protect 14 coal plants and 42 coal mines, alongside building two new coal plants and a major export terminal.
      • Permit Accelerations: The Department of the Interior approved 76 coal mining permits in under a month using emergency procedures, compared to zero coal leases approved during the prior four years.
      • Acreage Expansion: Opened 13.1 million acres of public lands for energy leasing (excluding national parks and wildlife refuges).
  • The West Gateway Export Terminal: Scheduled to break ground this summer, this long-delayed project will export over 12 million tons of clean coal per year by summer 2028, primarily targeting allies in the Pacific region.
  • Grid Reliability Metrics: Energy Secretary Chris Wright reported that preserving 17 gigawatts of coal power online successfully prevented grid failure and saved hundreds of lives during Winter Storm Fern on the East Coast.
  • AI Infrastructure Self-Generation: To protect the national grid from the massive energy demands of Artificial Intelligence (AI) data centers, the administration is permitting tech firms to build their own off-grid electric utility plants, with any excess power routed back into the public grid.

## Bilateral Trade and the Impending India Deal

  • U.S.-India Trade Negotiations: Confirmed that a major bilateral trade agreement with India is imminent following a letter delivered to Prime Minister Narendra Modi by Senator/Secretary Marco Rubio.
  • Tariff Reciprocity: Criticized India's historical trade policies, citing past instances of 200% tariffs on U.S. exports like Harley Davidson motorcycles while the U.S. charged zero tariffs on Indian imports.
      • Trump emphasized that reciprocal trade strategies are now generating significant revenue for the U.S., but maintained a strong relationship with PM Modi to secure the final deal.

## Geopolitical Strategy and Military Red Lines

  • Iran Ceasefire and Nuclear Red Line: Reaffirmed that the U.S. will not permit Iran to obtain nuclear weapons.
      • Trump highlighted that U.S. B-2 bombers completely obliterated deep Iranian nuclear facilities 10 months ago, and the sites are now actively monitored by nine Space Force satellite cameras.
      • Established a strict red line for the ceasefire: the conflict will immediately resume if Iran kills any U.S. troops.
  • Ukraine and Russia Peace Mediation: Welcomed a potential bilateral meeting between Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelenskyy and Russian President Vladimir Putin. Trump claimed his administration's proposed compromises are paving the way for a resolution.
  • Venezuela and Cuba Operations:
      • Venezuela: Declared the brief military intervention in Venezuela highly successful, noting that Venezuelan oil exports have tripled since the operation.
      • Cuba: Described Cuba as a collapsed state starving for resources after losing Venezuelan financial support. Trump promised to implement strategic economic investment programs to rebuild Cuba once the Iranian situation is fully resolved.
  • NATO and the Strait of Hormuz: Criticized NATO and European allies for refusing to assist with security in the Strait of Hormuz. Trump asserted the U.S. military successfully neutralized hostile forces independently, rendering European assistance unnecessary but noting that allies will face subsequent economic costs.

## Domestic Legislation, Electoral Reform, and Infrastructure

  • The Save America Act: Urged Congress to pass federal election standards requiring mandatory voter photo ID, proof of U.S. citizenship, and the elimination of mail-in ballots (except for cases of military service, travel, illness, or disability).
  • Monument Restoration and D.C. Infrastructure:
      • Waterfront Beautification: Completed the restoration of 22 historic Washington D.C. waterfalls and fountains, including the Columbus Fountain at Union Station.
      • The Lincoln Memorial Promenade: Announced the upcoming construction of a pedestrian promenade bridge over two major highways, finally connecting the Lincoln Memorial directly to the Potomac River in accordance with the 120-year-old McMillan Plan.
      • Triumphal Arch: Announced plans to build a Great Triumphal Arch in D.C. honoring military history, alongside the National Garden of American Heroes.
  • Economic Relief and Tax Deductions:
      • Middle-Class Deductions: Implemented a new federal income tax deduction for the interest paid on car loans, with the strict qualification that the vehicle must be manufactured in America.
      • Automotive Deregulation: Repealed the 2009 EPA endangerment finding and revoked California's EV mandate waivers, which EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin stated would reduce vehicle costs for consumers by an average of $2,400.

How Legora Went From YC to $100M ARR in 18 Months

Y Combinator(2.3M)·22:47·8.2K views·2026-06-05
Max JunestrandCo-Founder, Legora Gustaf AlströmerGeneral Partner, Y Combinator
TL;DRLegora (YC W24) scaled to over $100M ARR in just 18 months by bundling core legal workflows, leveraging aggressive sales tactics, and securing top-tier venture backing from Benchmark.
The company is now transitioning from task-augmentation features to fully autonomous AI...

TL;DR

Legora (YC W24) scaled to over $100M ARR in just 18 months by bundling core legal workflows, leveraging aggressive sales tactics, and securing top-tier venture backing from Benchmark. The company is now transitioning from task-augmentation features to fully autonomous AI agents as it aims to build Europe’s next giant tech conglomerate.

The Jude Law Campaign & Creative Brand Strategy

  • Disrupting Legal Tech Marketing: Legora wanted to move away from the traditional, dry advertising style of the legal tech industry to build a brand that felt exciting and modern.
  • Landing Jude Law: Legora spent six months chasing the actor, initially facing rejection due to Hollywood's broader skepticism toward AI.
      • The Pitch: Max Junestrand convinced him by sharing real customer testimonials from Legora's "Customer Love" Slack channel, showing how the product helped overworked lawyers get home to their families for the weekend.
      • High-End Production: To maintain his artistic identity, Law brought on an *Oppenheimer* cinematographer and a *Saturday Night Live* screenwriter to produce a highly polished, comedic commercial campaign.
  • Massive Local Impact: The campaign created immediate market saturation, generating "17 touchpoints" of visibility for travelers landing in Stockholm and securing high-quality, unexpected inbound leads.

The YC Grind and High-Intensity Sales Tactics

  • The Airbnb "Gulag": During the YC W24 batch, Legora’s 10-person team lived and worked out of a single Airbnb, working relentlessly to support global operations.
      • Extreme Schedules: Max and his co-founders ran sales calls from 1:00 AM to 10:00 AM daily to target international clients before sleeping a few hours and attending YC events.
  • Overcoming Conservative Buyers: Max drove early traction by personally pitching conservative Nordic law firms with intense, unapologetic enthusiasm.
      • Leveraging FOMO: By telling firms that their top competitors were already onboarded, Legora successfully convinced traditional buyers to embrace a fast-evolving, early-stage product.
  • Securing Benchmark Capital: Despite a subpar mock pitch at YC, the team excelled when it mattered, landing Benchmark partners Peter Fenton and Chetan.
      • Resilience to Rejection: Max emphasizes that founders must maintain absolute confidence in investor meetings, as VCs can instantly sense self-doubt when a company starts receiving "no's."

Product Bundling vs. Single-Feature Competitors

  • The Three-Pronged Manifesto: Legora’s early product strategy was built on a simple three-page document outlining superiority in three core areas:
      • An AI assistant/agent chat interface.
      • A comprehensive tabular agreement review tool.
      • A deep Microsoft Word add-in integration.
  • Defeating Incumbents with a Bundle: When Legora was at just $1M ARR, they competed against specialized companies doing nearly $50M ARR that only focused on one of these features (such as tabular review). By bundling all three seamlessly, Legora rapidly captured market share and churned their competitors' enterprise clients.
  • Top-Tier Talent Density: Legora has scaled from 40 to nearly 500 employees globally. A key driver of this execution speed is that 15% of the company's product and engineering organization consists of ex-YC founders.

The Evolution of AI Agents and Competing with Foundation Models

  • Transition to Autonomous Agents: Legora is shifting from individual task augmentation to deploying proactive, long-running agents.
      • M&A Due Diligence Example: Instead of helping a lawyer read a single document, Legora's agents can restructure an entire chaotic, unstructured M&A data room, run templated diligence queries, and highlight missing files autonomously over 20- to 30-minute processing runs.
  • The Six-Month "Code Lag": Legal tech agents trail software development agents (like Cursor or Claude Code) by roughly six months because code is binary and easier to evaluate out-of-the-box, giving Legora a clear roadmap of where agent capabilities are heading.
  • Defensibility Against OpenAI and Anthropic: Max compares the current AI landscape to the database and infrastructure wars between MongoDB and AWS.
      • Building Moats: Legora's defense against smarter foundation models relies on deep workflow integrations, proprietary enterprise data access, established user behaviors, and rapid distribution rather than the intelligence of the underlying raw model.

Long-Term Vision: Dropping the "Legal" from Legora

  • Conglomerate Ambitions: Legora's leadership views legal tech as "base camp." The long-term plan is to eventually drop "legal" from their identity and expand into a highly diversified tech conglomerate.
      • The Big Tech Blueprint: Max points to Google (using search ads to fund Waymo) and Facebook (using social media to fund Meta/VR) as inspirations for building a broad, multi-industry business.
      • A European Giant: Legora aims to break Europe's relative lack of massive tech companies by leveraging AI to democratize talent and scale globally from Sweden.

Notable Quotes

"Our product was frankly not that great, but they wanted to work with me and they wanted to work with us. Probably half of them probably thought I was on cocaine... I was like, 'This is the fucking future and you have to work with us and we're going to make you win. And by the way, the biggest firm in the Nordics already works with us, so if you don't, you're kind of a loser.'" — Max Junestrand
"One day we'd love to erase that 'legal' in front of 'legal tech' Legora... We want to create from Europe—I mean, the largest tech company we have is SAP. Like fucking SAP? Are you serious? And now I think there's a unique moment in time where the leverage and shift can happen." — Max Junestrand
"What is your moat when the models continue to get smarter? That’s the real underlying question here... What are the inputs and outputs, what is the proprietary data, what are the workflow moats, what is the behavior that we're teaching our users? And I think for Legora, we understood that we had to get big fast." — Max Junestrand

An example would be a large M&A transaction where there's like many different steps. You can break down the steps and take the due diligence part for instance, where you get the entire data room and then well, for often it's like completely unstructured. So the Legora agent now has access to tools where it can manipulate the file tree. So you can just tell it like, "Hey, structure the data room. Here's our template folder structure." And it's just like bip bop you know let me go do it. And then you tell it, "Okay it's this type of company, here's the type of diligence questions that we want to run through. And I think for Legora we understood that we had to get big fast.

Jamie Dimon called him ‘full of s--t.’ Brian Armstrong answers.

POLITICO(215.0K)·33:01·8.8K views·2026-06-05
Brian ArmstrongCEO, Coinbase Dasha BurnsReporter, POLITICO
TL;DRCoinbase CEO Brian Armstrong defends the crypto industry's push for clear regulatory frameworks under the Clarity Act, emphasizing that crypto's massive bipartisan grassroots base and highly funded PACs are successfully reshaping Washington's legislative and political landscape.

TL;DR

Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong defends the crypto industry's push for clear regulatory frameworks under the Clarity Act, emphasizing that crypto's massive bipartisan grassroots base and highly funded PACs are successfully reshaping Washington's legislative and political landscape.

***

The Clash Over the Clarity Act and Traditional Banking

  • The Regulatory Debate: The debate surrounding the Clarity Act centers on whether cryptocurrency platforms and classical banking should operate under the same regulatory rules, particularly regarding stablecoin rewards.
      • Stablecoin Rewards: Armstrong argues that stablecoin rewards (backed 100% by short-term US Treasuries) are low-risk and should be accessible to consumers. Traditional banks oppose this, citing concerns over "deposit flight" from traditional accounts to high-yielding stablecoins.
      • Legislative Compromise: In negotiations with the Senate Banking Committee, Coinbase conceded on a key bank lobby demand, agreeing that rewards would not be paid on idle consumer balances in order to keep the bill moving forward.
  • Bank Licenses vs. Trust Charters: Armstrong rejects the notion that Coinbase is lobbying for lighter regulations than traditional banks.
      • Fractional Reserve Lending: Traditional banks face heavy regulations because they engage in fractional reserve lending, which presents risks like bank runs.
      • 100% Reserves: Coinbase operates under different licenses (such as an OCC trust charter, CFTC, and SEC regulations) where they maintain 100% of customer reserves and do not perform fractional lending, rendering bank-specific rules unnecessary.
  • The Jamie Dimon Conflict: Addressing JP Morgan CEO Jamie Dimon’s public criticism of him, Armstrong called for direct communication over media hostility and emphasized that clear rules in America are ultimately beneficial for both banks and crypto firms.

Bipartisanship and the Grassroots Crypto Movement

  • Bipartisan Policy Motivations: Armstrong asserts that crypto has become a highly bipartisan issue in Washington, attracting lawmakers for different reasons:
      • Democratic Appeal: Focuses heavily on financial inclusion, aiming to democratize financial services for the estimated 80% of Americans who feel the current system does not work for them.
      • Republican Appeal: Emphasizes personal freedom, national security, and ensuring the U.S. dollar remains the global reserve currency in competition with China.
  • Grassroots and Generational Support: The political power of crypto is fueled by a rapidly growing demographic base.
      • User Base: Approximately 50 million Americans have used cryptocurrency.
      • Stand with Crypto: Over 3 million advocates have registered with StandwithCrypto.org to elect pro-crypto candidates, sending over 1 million direct messages to legislators.
      • Generational Divide: Crypto adoption is heavily concentrated in the 15-to-35 age demographic, with younger congressional staffers and family members frequently driving the education of older lawmakers.

Crypto's Expanding Political and Lobbying Influence

  • The Fairshake Super PAC: Coinbase and other crypto-backed entities have heavily funded Fairshake, an independent, bipartisan super PAC.
      • Financial War Chest: Fairshake entered the election cycle with more than $193 million in cash on hand, making it one of the most influential outside spending groups in politics.
  • The Political Cost of Anti-Crypto Stances: Armstrong highlighted how opposition to crypto has directly impacted major political races.
      • Sherrod Brown's Defeat: Armstrong noted that former Senate Banking Chairman Sherrod Brown consistently refused to meet with him or Ohio-based crypto entrepreneurs. Armstrong believes this resistance contributed significantly to Brown losing his Senate seat to Bernie Moreno.
      • New Committee Progress: Under new Senate Banking Chair Tim Scott, the committee has achieved more legislative progress in months than it had in several years prior.
  • Key Congressional Allies: Armstrong highlighted strong, collaborative relationships with lawmakers on both sides of the aisle, including Senators Kirsten Gillibrand, Angela Alsobrooks, and Cynthia Lummis.

Future Horizons: AI Regulation and Human-AI Integration

  • AI Regulation and Startups: Regarding President Trump’s recent executive order establishing a voluntary 30-day government review process for new AI models, Armstrong warned against excessive federal hurdles.
      • Stifling Innovation: Armstrong cautioned that mandatory registrations could stall the industry, drawing a parallel to how the creation of the Nuclear Regulatory Commission virtually halted the expansion of domestic nuclear energy.
  • The Convergence of Humanity and AI: Armstrong shared a highly contrarian, philosophical view on the long-term future of artificial intelligence.
      • Incremental Uploads: He argues humans are already incrementally merging with AI by outsourcing memory, organization, and communication to smartphones and cloud systems.
      • The 50-Year Vision: Armstrong envisions a future where brain-machine interfaces allow humans to control humanoid robotic bodies, potentially preserving a continuity of self even after the biological body ceases to function.

***

Notable Quotes

"We choose not to do fractional reserve lending, which is the part that would require a bank license... If banks wanted to get rid of their bank license and just operate the way we want to, then that's something they could do as well." — Brian Armstrong
"There's something like 80% of Americans feel that the current financial system's not working for them, and crypto is this great democratizing force in terms of access to financial services." — Brian Armstrong
"If we shed our mortal coil at some point, we will have merged with AI and still have continuity of self throughout that process." — Brian Armstrong

Hello, hello, and welcome to The Conversation. I'm Dasha Burns, and every week on this show, I talk to the most compelling and sometimes unexpected power players in Washington and beyond. Banks should have to compete on a level playing field, and if the American people feel that the banks are not paying high enough interest rates and stablecoin rewards can offer them more, then maybe the banks should have to pay higher interest rates to compete. Listen, to your point about the actual policy, I want to dig more into the point that Dimon and some of the banks are making here because there are a lot of questions about the implications here. Basically, for those that haven't been following this closely, the legislation is called the Clarity Act. Traditional banks are concerned that this legislation could allow crypto companies like Coinbase to offer customers rewards that look and feel a lot like interest on a paid savings account without having to follow the same rules that banks do and be backed by similar protections. I'm curious, what's your response to that? Why shouldn't crypto companies have to submit to the same rules as banks and be backed by similar protections? Well, yeah, okay. So on the stablecoin reward issue, in my view, customers ideally would just be able to earn these rewards on any balance. That's actually what got passed into law by a bipartisan vote in Congress with the Genius Act just not that long ago, and I think that's a good thing. Why shouldn't Americans just be able to earn more money on their money? Stablecoins under the Genius Act are backed by short-term US Treasuries. It's quite low risk; it's 100% reserves. And that's what Congress came to as a decision. So going into the market structure legislation, I was sort of assuming that was a settled matter. Why would Congress reopen something that it had just passed into law three months ago? But the bank lobby kind of came back quite strongly and said, "Actually, we don't think that that's a good thing." It was a little unclear to me why. They sort of made this argument about deposit flight, even though there's been no evidence of any deposit flight as stablecoins have grown. Actually, the bank deposits have also grown. Maybe they have a conflict of interest on that, or they're worried about something else. But putting that aside for a moment, the Senate Banking Committee sort of came to us and said, "Alright, see if you can go meet their ask. Find a way for you guys to solve this where idle balances cannot pay rewards, but we're not going to get rid of this entire concept of Americans being able to earn more rewards on their money." And so that's exactly what we did. We conceded that point in our negotiation with them. We said, "Alright, if you don't want rewards paid on idle balances, that is something we don't like. We don't think it's good for America, but if that's what gets this done, maybe we can live with it." And for some of them, that wasn't enough, despite all the other pieces that they got as well. So, yeah, that's where it landed. Ultimately, the Senate came to this negotiated compromise, and I think that's what we've got to stick with at this point. But at sort of the high-level, bird's-eye view, you know, last year you said you want Coinbase to be a bank replacement for people. Ultimately, we want to be a bank replacement for people. We want to be their primary financial account. We do want to become a super app and provide all types of financial services. We want to become people's primary financial account. Why should crypto firms get lighter rules than the banks do for offering similar services? How do you reconcile that? So we're not asking for lighter rules, actually. When you build a financial services company, you can choose what kind of license you want to go get, and that limits you on certain products that you can offer, right? So for instance, if you want to get a bank license, you can do that. The thing that that allows you to do is fractional reserve lending. That basically means that when you deposit funds at a bank, they don't have all of it there. They've lent it out to other people, and they keep the margin for that. They don't necessarily pass along a huge percentage of it to you—typically zero in your checking account, a couple of basis points in your savings account—and they keep what's called the net interest margin, right? And there's all kinds of regulation that comes with that because it's dangerous to do fractional reserve lending, at least in my view. You can occasionally get a run on the bank and these kinds of things. And so if you don't want to do that, you can get other types of licenses. For instance, we have an OCC trust charter, which is another federal regulator, but we keep 100% of reserves. So that's a custodian license where we keep 100% of the reserves. There's no such thing as fractional reserve lending there. But you can still do payments, you can pay rewards to customers, and you can allow customers to opt in to certain products, even around lending and things like that. We have other licenses we work with on the trading side of our business as well at the federal level and at the state level. So the CFTC and the SEC regulate those. We have dozens of financial service regulators that we work with in the United States. We choose not to do fractional reserve lending, which is the part that would require a bank license. I don't think that's a necessary thing for us to do. So, yeah, if banks wanted to get rid of their bank license and just operate the way we do, then that's something they could do as well. You know, I think one of the other factors here that's raised some eyebrows in Washington is the power of the crypto lobby. I believe they sent about a million contacts to legislators on just this one particular issue recently. And so, the financial system isn't working for a lot of them. This year, we've already seen some interesting trends when it comes to tech, crypto, and AI. Tech-backed candidates across California just recently struck out in the primaries this week, including former tech entrepreneur Matt Mahan's bid for LA Mayor. Mahan was largely supported by Silicon Valley. You actually went to high school with Mahan, my producers tell me. The votes are still being counted right now, but he's likely to finish in the single digits. Do you think there's anything you can take from that loss? What lessons might you be able to take there? Yeah, I mean, I think the votes are still being counted, but I'm a fan of Matt Mahan. I think he's a sensible candidate. Not just because I went to high school with him, although I guess that gives you some game film on who that person actually is. But, yeah, I thought he was a good candidate. Still, the people ultimately are going to vote in different ways, so I think everyone's watching to see which way California's going to go. It feels like crypto has become mainstream in Washington faster than it has on Main Street. A recent Politico poll found that most Americans have never bought crypto, don't plan to, trust banks more than crypto firms, and don't want Congress to prioritize crypto over other issues like housing. It also comes after the 2022 FTX collapse that you mentioned before. Do you think the industry has failed to make a compelling case to the average American about why they should care about crypto, and what can you do about that? No, I don't think so. I mean, it's still early with crypto. You have to remember we've been operating in an environment for years now where there was active hostility in some cases—like with Gary Gensler, regulation by enforcement, one might say. And now, we're just on the cusp of, hopefully, getting some clear rules on the books to go build this industry. So it's still very early days and, despite that, there's about 50 million Americans who have used crypto at some point. By the way, crypto goes through ups and downs. People at various points are super excited about it, and other times they're not as excited about it. That's okay. Actually, I think crypto is well beyond what most people think of it now. A lot of people think about trading Bitcoin, Ethereum, and memecoins. Crypto's actually updating all aspects of the financial system. Stablecoin payments, for instance, have been growing like wildfire over the last few years, and it's just a faster, cheaper way to move money for the entire economy. It reduces friction, etc. Other asset classes are coming on-chain, like tokenized equities, commodities, and real-world assets. All the things people are talking about now, like these IPOs that are coming up that are really massive, people are trading perpetual futures on those. The AI boom is leading to all kinds of trades that people are making around energy, chips, the Strait of Hormuz, and oil. Every asset class is coming on-chain from a trading point of view. Stablecoin payments is the next big use case, and then we're seeing others like prediction markets, borrowing, and lending grow really nicely. So this is going to ultimately update all aspects of the financial system. In any given moment, people might be up or down on Bitcoin, but that's just a small part of the overall picture. Well, and politics plays a huge role. I've been fascinated to see what a big player Coinbase has become in the political space. I think that's a good thing to have out there. That just speaks to this large grassroots movement that I mentioned earlier and the bipartisan support that exists there politically in Congress. I mean, it's pretty remarkable. The super PAC had more than $193 million cash on hand at the start of 2026 to influence the cycle. You can do a lot with that kind of cash. Honestly, he refused to take a meeting with me. I think five times I flew to DC and tried to meet with him, but could never get a meeting. I haven't had that conversation yet with my policy team, but I think as it gets closer and we see who the likely candidates are, I would certainly make some donation decisions based on how pro-crypto they were. I think that makes sense for me as the CEO of Coinbase. Before I let you go, I do have to ask you about AI because you're really bullish about using it at Coinbase. This week President Trump signed an executive order asking some AI companies to submit their new models to a voluntary government review 30 days before releasing the products to the public. It was delayed after a bunch of back-and-forth in the White House and some pushback from the industry, but ultimately he did sign. Do you support that executive order? Do you think that's a good thing? I haven't read that exact executive order, so I don't want to comment on it specifically. But I would just say about AI in general, there are certainly national security issues that many people have talked about. I don't need to go into that; it makes sense to me why the government would have to have insight into what's happening and think about export controls and all kinds of things. I also think it's dangerous to think about having to register every model with the government or something like that. That would just slow down innovation. A lot of these companies are building on the frontier in a way that you don't want to have the government picking winners and losers. We've seen examples of that in the past where too much regulation can really slow down an industry. Like when the Nuclear Regulatory Commission got created, I think there was almost zero nuclear power plants created ever after that, right? So if we're not careful, sometimes these things can kill startups and future innovation. What's really leading to America's strength is being a leader on the technology and financial services front. To bring it back to the Clarity Act, that's exactly what we're trying to do here to get that built in America, which benefits Americans and America in many different ways. Big picture though, without—I know you haven't seen the text of the EO, but it's a voluntary review of AI models. Do you think that's a good idea? I don't know enough to comment again without having seen the text. I think if it's voluntary, that sounds better than mandatory. But the devil's in the details, right? You said something about AI on another podcast recently that I've really been thinking about. "I'm okay with the idea of like AI superseding humanity in a way. Like we should try to merge with that and evolve ourselves, but we should also be proud to be like the birth of that if it really goes that far." Can you explain what you mean by that? Your producer did their homework. I like it. Yeah, so I mean, I think maybe this is one of my more contrarian ideas, but I think that we all as human beings are going through a little bit of coming to terms or going through the stages of grief or cope—whatever you want to call it. We look at how first it was Deep Blue playing chess, and then it was AlphaGo, and then it was self-driving cars, and now we're seeing it be able to write better code than most people or even solve these math and physics proofs. And so I think we're starting to wonder, is there anything that AI is not going to surpass us on? I might be in the minority, but I think it doesn't seem to be an end to that. It's sort of a question of whether that will be in one year, ten years, or fifty years, but it seems unlikely to me that AI won't continue to evolve beyond us in most things. So, one way to think of that is to be threatened by it. Another way to think of it is to say, "Alright, well how can that make the world better, right?" We all rely on various people in different places—very intelligent beings at various places around the world running things. There's lots of science fiction that's been written about this, like the Culture series. There's a whole concept of how you merge with AI, like brain-machine interfaces and things like that. But in many ways, we're already merged with AI. People don't think about that, but how often are you interfacing with your phone today? How much does your phone know about you? What music you like, what shows you want to watch on YouTube, how you might type this next email. To put a maybe point on it, our brains are already somewhat being uploaded to the cloud over time. It just makes us more efficient. It's a little bit like how we created global connectivity and get more work done, and I'm not worried about forgetting things anymore because I can write them down in my to-do app. That's a simple example, but in the future we're just going to get increased connectivity with that and I think it will mostly be a very positive thing for society. So that was what I was trying to provoke the thought for folks. Yeah, I mean, put your science fiction hat on for a second. When you talk about that merge 10, 20, 50 years from now, what does that look like in your mind's eye? Yeah, I think it probably happens gradually, not suddenly. Just imagine for a moment that you were able to get some sort of brain-machine interface that allowed you to look up things on Google or send a message to someone. Okay, so that might start to feel quite normal over time. And okay, now imagine that just by thinking, you could also control a humanoid robot body. Okay, that's interesting. I wouldn't use it all the time, but maybe there's some instance where that's a useful thing to do. Okay, and imagine you now could control ten or a hundred robot bodies, and they're all in different places around the world or maybe even on different planets, exploring the solar system. And at some point, you know, your human body gets old and dies—which, by the way, we're also working on longevity drugs, so maybe that won't happen. But if one out of your hundred bodies went away, you'd still feel like you, right? It's a little bit like—to make a gruesome example—if you got into an accident and you lost one of your arms, you still feel like you. It's an appendage. And so if we're able to have a greater sense of self over time through humanoid robot bodies or these brain-machine interfaces, I think if we shed our mortal coil at some point, we will have merged with AI and still have continuity of self throughout that process. You're starting to get into some pretty philosophical topics that I didn't know if we were going to go there on a political podcast, but there we are. Meta AI Chief Wang on Winning the Race in AI

Bloomberg Live(189.0K)·20:23·39.7K views·2026-06-05
Alexandr WangChief AI Officer, Meta Kurt WagnerReporter, Bloomberg
TL;DRAt Bloomberg Tech 2026, Meta’s Chief AI Officer Alexandr Wang discussed the company's aggressive transition toward frontier AI capabilities, highlighting the success of its "Muse Spark" model and its upcoming competitive "entree" model.
He addressed Meta's pivot away from...

TL;DR

At Bloomberg Tech 2026, Meta’s Chief AI Officer Alexandr Wang discussed the company's aggressive transition toward frontier AI capabilities, highlighting the success of its "Muse Spark" model and its upcoming competitive "entree" model. He addressed Meta's pivot away from blanket open-sourcing due to bio-risk triggers and outlined a future where highly personal, multimodal AI agents redefine human-technology interaction.

Meta's Scaling Strategy and the Transition to Frontier Models

  • Rebuilding the Scaling Ladder: Over the past year, Meta Superintelligence Labs has been building a new scaling ladder, developing fresh infrastructure, and executing deep research to establish a highly predictable scaling curve for its models.
  • The "Appetizer" vs. the "Entree" Model:
      • Muse Spark: Released in April, this model served as an initial data point on Meta's new scaling trajectory. While not at the tier of leading frontier models, its public reception and integration into Meta AI drove record-breaking user adoption.
      • The Upcoming Frontier Model: Meta is currently training its next-generation model (referred to metaphorically as the "entree"). Wang noted that early training results are highly promising and expects the model to compete directly with the world’s leading frontier AI systems.
  • Key Barriers to Scale: To successfully transition from Muse Spark to the frontier level, Meta is actively focused on scaling compute, expanding high-quality training data, and pioneering new baseline research breakthroughs.

The Evolving Open-Source Strategy and Safety Concerns

  • The Shift from Open Source: Unlike previous models like LLaMA, Muse Spark was not open-sourced. Meta has shifted from a default open-source strategy to a more conditional deployment framework.
  • Safety Triggers and Bio-Risk:
      • Meta utilized its updated *Advanced AI Scaling Framework* and published its findings in a preparedness report.
      • During early training, Muse Spark triggered elevated risk markers, specifically in the area of bio-risk.
  • The Challenge of Open-Source Mitigations: Wang explained that safety mitigations are highly manageable when a model is hosted within proprietary products (like Meta AI). However, once a model is open-sourced, Meta loses the ability to prevent downstream misuse in unmonitored environments.
  • Future Open-Source Initiatives: Meta is currently developing separate, specialized models designed specifically to be safe for open-source release while retaining as much performance capability as possible.

Model Strengths and the Rise of Personal AI Agents

  • Muse Spark's Core Strengths: Despite its smaller size, Muse Spark demonstrated exceptional, unexpected capabilities in key areas:
      • Multimodality: Seamlessly handling video, image, and audio formats.
      • Healthcare: Exhibiting highly robust performance in health-related domains.
      • Coding: Generating functional code, interactive mini-games, and digital artifacts.
  • The Paradigm of Personal AI Agents: Wang envisions a future where consumers rely on a small handful (1 to 2) of highly trusted personal agents, rather than an ecosystem of hundreds of specialized apps.
      • Consumer Agents: Meta is currently "cooking" its next consumer agent designed to manage personal relationships, health, and parenting.
      • Business Agents: Meta recently announced a business agent allowing advertisers to automate customer service and eventually help build ad campaigns.
  • Privacy and Public Trust: Acknowledging Meta's history, Wang stated that establishing deep trust and agentic safety is an industry-wide hurdle that represents a fundamental redefinition of the human relationship with technology.

Geopolitics, Jobs, and the Regulatory Landscape

  • The US-China AI Race: Wang emphasized that the United States currently leads China in AI capabilities, but stressed that maintaining this lead is a critical geopolitical priority.
  • AI's Impact on the Economy and Employment:
      • Job Displacement: Wang acknowledged the internal pain of recent layoffs at Meta, noting the complexity of managing a large organization during a major technological shift.
      • The Entrepreneurial Boom: Despite fears of job losses, Meta’s internal data shows that AI tools are driving unprecedented rates of small business creation and entrepreneurship globally.
  • AI Regulation: Wang expressed support for the Trump administration's recent executive order requiring pre-release model reviews, noting that while regulation is notoriously difficult to get right, oversight is necessary as models become exponentially more powerful.

Notable Quotes

"When we launch a model like Muse Spark in a product, we have a lot of ways to mitigate some of these risks and ensure that we're able to launch it in a safe and responsible way. It's much harder to do that when you open source a model and people can use that model in all sorts of contexts that we may not have full understanding of." — *Alexandr Wang*
"I use an agent to help me be healthier, and I use an agent to help me keep in touch with my friends and ensure that I maintain those relationships... Having an agent that's there to help support you do a good job of those things has been pretty transformational." — *Alexandr Wang*
"If you look at the history of civilization, technological advances are very important for countries or civilizations to adapt to and be able to adopt. And that really defines, truly, the course of history over a long arc. So I think it's very important that the United States is able to lead on AI." — *Alexandr Wang*
It was almost exactly a year ago that you started at Meta, leading the AI team, and I thought maybe you could set the scene for us just a little bit. I, I'm wondering if you can let us know where you think Meta is as an AI company today versus where it was a year ago, specifically. Like, what's the reputation of Meta as an AI company today versus a year ago when you got there? Yeah. I mean, I think it's been a very exciting year for, for Meta. And I mean, I think when, you know, roughly a year ago, a little more than a year ago, Meta released Llama 4. And while it was still an exciting release, I think, you know, it wasn't quite on the trajectory that Meta needed to be able to continue building a lot of the products and experiences that it seeked to build. And so, you know, we've been hard at work over the past year since I joined and since starting Meta Superintelligence Labs. You know, we've been undergoing an entire process of building a new scaling ladder for our models, developing new sets of infrastructure, and new research to feel the power of a new series and family of models. And you know, back in April, we released the very first fruits of that labor, you know, the new Spark models and an update to Meta AI. The reception to all of that was incredibly positive. It was even better than, frankly, we had expected internally. We saw incredible gains in usage of Meta AI. It was, you know, at the top of many of the app stores. And, you know, we're sort of working on our even larger models today and are excited about what we'll be able to demonstrate to the world. We clearly, you know, are on a very exciting and fast trajectory, and we're excited to continue showing the world what we produce. I think that, you know, the AI industry has gotten very hot and competitive obviously, in the past year as well. And, you know, we take that very seriously, but we're really excited. Yeah. Obviously, the goal you're spending in the same way that an OpenAI, Anthropic, and others are spending—do you feel like you guys are in that same tier as Meta? You know, is it OpenAI, Anthropic, Meta at this point, or do you feel like there's still a bit of a gap? Because I'd say certainly a year ago that was the perception. Yeah, we don't... I mean, the new Spark model that we released is not at the tier of the leading frontier models, but we believe it's a very exciting data point on the trajectory, and we expect the upcoming models we release to be quite competitive with the leading models in the world. Yeah. You called Muse Spark an appetizer, an appetizer model. To build that metaphor out, when does the entree model get here? You know, will it be at that tier that you guys ultimately want to be at? Yeah, we are, we are in the process of cooking. The... we're said the whole conversation is just going to be this, this back and forth. Yeah, yeah. No, no, I mean, we're, we're cooking it. We're excited to show it to the world once it's ready. We're seeing very exciting and promising results in the process of training it, right now, so we're quite excited about it. And, and I think just, you know, overall, we built the entire research effort around predictable scaling. So the entire belief of our overall research effort was, you know, that in many ways, the central belief behind the current modern AI boom is that as you scale these models, you will see and get incredible results and get predictable levels of increased capability. And so, you know, Muse Spark was an early data point on that scaling ladder for us. The next models we release will be an even greater point on the scaling curves, and we are really excited to show the world what we will be able to produce. And what... obviously, you went from basically rebuilding the team, rebuilding the lab to this Muse Spark model in a very short amount of time. What is the biggest barrier from getting to that... the appetizer to the next level? Is it... I assume it's not resources. You guys are spending a ton of money on this, on this effort. Is it simply time? Is it talent? Like, what's going to bring your models to that... that frontier? Yeah, it's, you know, we, we've talked about some of this in some of our public blog posts and whatnot, but it's about continuing to scale the data, the compute going into the models, as well as continuing to scale with research. So, continuing to drive advances in, you know, underlying research breakthroughs to continue driving forward the progress in the models, and building infrastructure to support all of this. You know, this is in many ways a year where all of them are dramatically scaling up their models. And we are on, we think, a much faster trajectory to do so, because obviously, we've been doing all this work over the course of the past year. But yeah, we need to build the infrastructure, we need to scale the data, scale the compute, train these large models, and show them to the world. Yeah, I want to talk about model strategy a little bit. Before you got to Meta, everything was open source. That was definitely the overarching strategy. The new Spark model is not open source. I believe I heard you on a prior interview basically say that as you guys were testing it, it didn't feel safe to open source. Can you go deeper on sort of what you mean by that and how you made that decision? Yeah. So one of the things that we did when, as part of Meta Superintelligence Labs, we updated our what we call our advanced AI scaling framework, which is really our view of, you know, what are the risks that we see in developing these very powerful models? And how do we want to handle those risks as we see them in early testing? And we published a lot of what we saw in the process of creating Muse Spark in our preparedness report. And some of the things that we saw was that it actually triggered some high-risk areas in the course of early training, particularly run-by risk, but also a number of the risks were elevated. And, you know, this is something I think the entire industry has seen as the models have improved pretty dramatically over the past year. So we certainly aren't the only ones to see a host of these risks show up as we scaled up the models and as we sort of kept pushing the frontiers of research. But we saw these risks trigger and we realized, you know, I think fundamentally, when we launch a model like Muse Spark in a product, we have a lot of ways to mitigate some of these risks and ensure that we're able to launch it in a safe and responsible way. It's much harder to do that when you open source a model and, you know, people can use that model in all sorts of contexts that we may not have full understanding of. So we're in the process right now of developing models that we believe are fit and safe to be open source while still maintaining as much of the performance capabilities as possible. So you will still do open source. It sounds like Llama, though, is not the brand or the pillar that you're going to do. Is everything open source going to be Muse Spark or adjacent? You know, we... we have exciting debates about branding internally, and nothing to share right now, but... yeah, okay. The big models that are coming that you've hinted at—give us a general sense. Obviously, you know, each model, each company is perhaps known for certain things. Do you feel as though you're moving in a direction where Meta's models are going to be known for, you know, best in class at X versus Y? Like, what are you hoping to accomplish with what you guys come out with next? Yeah. So already in Muse Spark, some areas where we were really impressed by the capabilities, even though it is, again, like a much smaller model than ultimately we intend to train, were around multimodality capability. So its ability to handle images, video, audio, and that's obviously very important and critical for Meta's business. Also, its capabilities in health were really impressive. And that was very exciting for us. You know, health is an area that we view as really critical as we scale these models out to billions of people all around the world. And then also, a lot of the early results we saw in the ability of the model to create your vibe code and create little games or artifacts or whatnot were very powerful. So, we are doubling down on some of these and continuing to invest into the capabilities of the model. So we're really excited for the upcoming models' release to be very, very capable agents, paired with a lot of these other strengths around multimodality, around health, and many others. And ultimately, what we're really excited to build for the world are the best personal agents for everybody around the world as much as possible. I want to get to agents in just a second because you just made some news on agents, actually, yesterday. You have other stuff in the works that we can talk about.
