If you look at the history of civilization, you know, technological advances are very important for countries or civilizations to adapt to and be able to adopt, and that really defines, you know, truly the course of history over a long arc. So I think it's very important that the United States is able to lead on AI. And that's a huge part of our focus at Meta, as well as ensuring that we are able to contribute to the United States leading. Where do you think we are right now as a country? Is the US leading? I think right now the US is leading, yes. And I think it's, you know, this is one of these situations where it's important for us always to track progress from many other countries, but especially China. You know, be very thoughtful and understand exactly what's happening within each country and what are the reasons those things are happening. But I think right now we're ahead. And what... sorry, I said I was going to get to agents, but one more: what could put that at risk? What could put that lead at risk? What's the most threatening thing to stop that? That's a good question. I mean, ultimately, I think we are in a phase where the research advancements in the industry that we're seeing from continuing to scale these models, apply more compute, apply more data to these models, are just incredibly exciting. And, you know, in some ways, the progress and pace of research today is nearly miraculous. And so, I think it's important that we're able to continue this pace of progress, that we're able to continue, you know, being able to continue scaling for these models. Yeah. Agents. Now, finally, you guys just yesterday, I believe it was, announced a business agent so advertisers can use this to, you know, interact with customers, I presume, eventually help even develop ad campaigns, things like that. But you're also developing a consumer agent. Talk me through your vision for how ultimately agents will reach all of us. Like, I guess I'm wondering, is an agent going to be similar to like my email address where I have one core agent and maybe a secondary agent? Or is it going to be like the apps on my phone where I have one agent for every single task in my life? Like, what do you envision we're going to be using as a society? Yeah, I, you know, we ultimately think it'll probably land somewhere in between those. I think, you know, we really believe that people are probably going to have one, maybe two, maybe a small handful of agents that they rely on. And maybe they have a personal agent that's focused on things like their health and maintaining their personal relationships, and, you know, helping them be a better parent and be better with their friends and family. And then, you know, perhaps they use that same agent in their work lives, especially if they're working in a small business or they're an entrepreneur, or working within a smaller organization. And then, you know, maybe there's worlds where if you work within a larger enterprise or a larger company, then these become bifurcated and separated. Not too much unlike email, let's say, or plenty of other key technologies that we use from day-to-day basis. So, yeah, we think that ultimately agents will be something that become deeply personal, and should be things where over time you find yourself being able to rely on them more and more and more for more and more of your personal life, more of your work life. And that'll be a process that, you know, all of society goes through together. Do you feel that Meta, in particular—and again, a lot of this happened before you got there, but a long history of privacy-related issues—are people going to be willing to trust a Meta agent with the personal tasks of their life that you're describing? Yeah. I think that this is like one of the most important societal questions for agents writ large. I mean, I think that there's an incredible amount of innovation and technology that we built out on things like agentic safety, you know, ensuring that these agents are respectful of your privacy, ensuring that they're respectful of your boundaries, and continuing to design products in a way that are able to support that. So, this is definitely something that we're taking very, very seriously, and we're being quite thoughtful about. And ultimately, we're excited to, you know, show the world what we've built. But, yeah, we think that this is not even just a Meta problem. This is an industry-wide problem. As we build more and more powerful agents, I think that it is a redefinition of humans' relationship with technology in many ways. And that's something that, you know, we're all going to have to think through and work through together. How soon will we see a Meta consumer agent? We are actually cooking it, cooking the entree. Yeah. But no, this is like, I think, one of the things that was very exciting for us internally about the new Spark launch in my eyes—that, you know, even when those launched, we were cooking things internally, both the larger models as well as some of these products that you referred to that we're, if anything, more excited about than what we came out with in April. And I think it's, you know, it's—no, I think it's very well known that this is a challenging thing to go through as a team and something that, you know, is important to acknowledge. Meta’s $200 AI Agent ‘Hatch’, Sam Altman-back Helion Valuation Hits $15.5B, Snowflake’s New AI Tools

The Information(21.5K)·43:39·1.1K views·2026-06-05
Jason DeanSan Francisco Bureau Chief, The Information Akash PasrichaHost, TITV
TL;DRMeta is internally planning a consumer-focused AI agent named "Hatch" targeting a $200-a-month premium tier, while Sam Altman-backed fusion energy startup Helion has raised $465 million at a $15.5 billion valuation to accelerate its power plant manufacturing.

TL;DR

Meta is internally planning a consumer-focused AI agent named "Hatch" targeting a $200-a-month premium tier, while Sam Altman-backed fusion energy startup Helion has raised $465 million at a $15.5 billion valuation to accelerate its power plant manufacturing.

***

Meta’s Premium AI Agent: "Hatch"

  • Consumer-Friendly OpenDevin: Meta is internally discussing a new AI agent tool called Hatch, designed to be a user-friendly, consumer-and-prosumer alternative to the complex OpenDevin platform.
      • Features: Hatch can write code and execute basic agentic tasks such as email management and calendar scheduling. Because Meta lacks dedicated email or calendar programs, the tool is being engineered to operate off-platform.
  • Pricing and Tiered Model: Internal documents indicate Meta is considering a premium tier priced at $199.99 per month to provide higher usage limits, alongside a baseline free version.
  • Release Timeline: Originally targeted for April and then delayed to July, the official release date remains fluid but is tentatively expected by mid-to-late summer.
  • Underlying Infrastructure: During current testing, Hatch relies on Anthropic’s Claude models. Meta aims to eventually migrate the tool to its own foundation models to avoid the high costs of operating a third-party wrapper.

Helion’s $15.5B Valuation & Fusion Scaling

  • Massive Series G Funding: Helion Energy raised $465 million in a round led by Thrive Capital, boosting the company’s valuation to $15.5 billion—nearly triple its valuation from the previous year.
  • The "TinyMerge" Strategy: Helion is running a sub-scale testbed program called "TinyMerge" in parallel with its main Orion power plant facility. This allows engineers to rapidly iterate on electronics and materials while training the world's first fusion power plant operators.
  • Vertical Integration Focus: To bypass supply chain bottlenecks and control costs, Helion is vertically integrating key component manufacturing.
      • Capacitors & Electronics: The company manufactures its own energy-storage capacitors and power electronics, which represent approximately 50% of a fusion power plant's total cost.
  • Commercial and Regulatory Progress: Helion continues to de-risk its path to market by securing environmental and building permits for its Malaga, Washington site and maintaining its landmark Power Purchase Agreement (PPA) with Microsoft.

Netskope’s AI Security & Financial Outlook

  • Financial Performance: Cloud security firm Netskope reported a slight top-line deceleration with 28% revenue growth this quarter. However, the company expanded ARR by 29%, achieved record gross retention, and grew new customer logos by nearly 60% year-over-year.
  • Sales Expansion Friction: The top-line deceleration is attributed to a massive, ongoing sales team expansion where roughly half of the sales reps are still in their training/ramping phase.
  • Frontier Model Testing: Netskope has early access to Anthropic's Mythos model and OpenAI's Daybreak program (GPT-5.5) to run vulnerability-detection harnesses on its own codebases prior to product releases.
  • Democratic Access vs. Safety: CEO Sanjay Beri emphasized the tension in releasing powerful frontier models like Mythos, advocating for eventual democratization to protect infrastructure, while acknowledging the risk of advanced models falling into the hands of malicious actors.

Snowflake’s Co-pilot and Co-work Rollouts

  • AI Product Launches: At its annual summit, Snowflake announced new capabilities for its personal assistant, Snowflake Co-work (including Deep Search, Artifacts, and Skill Catalog), and its coding agent, Snowflake Co-pilot, which now supports cross-platform integrations on mobile, desktop, Excel, and Cloud Code.
  • The Choice Architecture: Rather than restricting users to a single model, Snowflake partners with multiple providers—including Anthropic, OpenAI, and open-source options—allowing customers to choose the most cost-effective model for their specific task.
  • Customer Adoption: Over 13,600 customers actively use Snowflake’s AI capabilities weekly, with more than 7,200 utilizing Cortex Co-pilot.
  • Overcoming Adoption Barriers: Chief Data and AI Officer Anahita Tafvizi identified *trust* (ensuring deterministic accuracy and role-based access governance) and *cost predictability* as the two main barriers preventing wider corporate AI adoption.

The Push to Keep Top AI Minds in Academia

  • The Academic Brain Drain: Databricks and Perplexity co-founder Andy Konwinski is actively urging PhD candidates and researchers to reject multi-million dollar corporate compensation packages and remain in university research for a few years to secure fundamental breakthroughs.
  • The Problem with Closed Research: Frontier labs (such as Google, OpenAI, and Anthropic) are increasingly keeping their training data, computing scales, and software details secret, making scientific replication impossible for academic institutions.
  • The Lotte Institute: To combat this, Konwinski co-founded the Lotte Institute (a 501(c)(3) organization) in June 2025, committing $100 million of his own wealth. The institute funds 70 projects with grants of up to $10 million across top-tier universities including Stanford, Berkeley, MIT, and Carnegie Mellon.

***

Notable Quotes

"Mark Zuckerberg has been very, very open about the fact that they're looking for new revenue streams. They have a lot of ambition around agents... but this idea of doing something that would range off platform, that would have much greater functionality than anything that they offer really at the moment is new territory." — Jason Dean, San Francisco Bureau Chief at *The Information*
"Enterprises are in the stage of realizing that AI moved farther than security did. So security's catching up." — Sanjay Beri, Founder and CEO of Netskope
"When I have a lot of conversation with the customers, it really boils down to two primary concerns. One is trust and one is cost." — Anahita Tafvizi, Chief Data and AI Officer at Snowflake
"He [Andy Konwinski] was arguing that it's fundamental to democracy to have an ecosystem of open research. And the fewer people who stay in academia... the fewer people in the next generation will be qualified to take on these roles at frontier labs and lead the biggest companies." — Laura Bratton, Applied AI Reporter at *The Information*

We've reported in The Information that using Mythos is very expensive. Have you burned through your allotted budget for using Mythos? Has it been more expensive than you thought? I'm not measuring my people on token maxing; I'm measuring them on outcomes and outputs—you know, PRs for engineers, output for marketing. And so, have I exceeded what I thought I would have a year ago? Of course. Most people didn't budget for what they thought and what they're seeing now in terms of their token spend, and so you have to adjust. The reality is that most companies, including us, probably have less open headcount, right, for things like R&D, and they're building these smaller agile teams and leveraging AI and tokens, frontier models like Mythos, but also for development more. Do you think that Mythos should be released to the public at some point? It's a great question, and I go back and forth on this. You don't rely on one platform for all security and networking. Same thing. You don't want to rely necessarily on one model as you find vulnerabilities. But sometimes— Which is more? That's a good question. I'd have to—I mean, the reality is, look, there's a new frontier model every four to six weeks. That's the reality. And so what you have, I think, is this constant, like, one gets ahead, another tries to catch up, and so on. And so at any one point, you know, one can be, and the other can be in another point. Is it that you'll just see more customers saying we need more protection, or do you see a way to incorporate Mythos into your own products and have a business impact there? It's a great question. So if you look at what we do, we're not a code scanner, right, or vulnerability scanner. We live on the opposite side of the spectrum of security. We basically live in the world where you assume there are vulnerabilities, and what we do is we are the highway for all your traffic. So when an agent talks to a cloud app or gets data, makes a tool call, when a user prompts something, we look at every prompt. We look at every tool call. We make sure: is that agent supposed to do that? Are they supposed to have access to that data? Is that user's prompt really appropriate? And does this response have malware? And so for us, we're the real-time governance, and as a result, with Mythos and with the explosion of AI agents, what we're seeing is we're processing trillions and trillions of AI transactions. And we're guardrailing them, protecting them, and ultimately enabling AI to be used safely without people shutting it down. And so that's kind of the way we see it. And Mythos for us, obviously, helps internally. Satya Nadella on making human and token capital compound

Reid Hoffman(110.0K)·60:17·2.9K views·2026-06-05
Satya NadellaCEO, Microsoft Reid HoffmanCo-founder, LinkedIn and Host
TL;DRThe future of corporate competitiveness depends on a firm's ability to compound the returns of human capital alongside "token capital"—the proprietary, tacit knowledge of an enterprise encoded into digital workflows.
To capture this value safely, organizations must shift...

TL;DR

The future of corporate competitiveness depends on a firm's ability to compound the returns of human capital alongside "token capital"—the proprietary, tacit knowledge of an enterprise encoded into digital workflows. To capture this value safely, organizations must shift from basic chatbot interfaces to highly auditable agentic development environments while ensuring AI infrastructure is optimized for economic and token efficiency.

The Dual Engine of the Firm: Human and Token Capital

  • Defining Token Capital: The value of a modern firm will increasingly be defined by the compounding interplay between its human workers and its "token capital."
      • Tacit Knowledge Leakage: A firm’s unique judgment, taste, and operational history constitute its tacit knowledge. If enterprises do not build self-controlled loops to capture this data, they risk leaking their IP to external foundation model companies that employ former staff to train public models.
  • The "Hill-Climbing Machine": Nadella frames the modern equivalent of Microsoft’s original BASIC interpreter as a "hill-climbing machine"—the software system an enterprise uses to input proprietary evals, metrics, and data to continuously train and optimize models inside its own boundary.
  • Preventing One-Way Doors: To maintain strategic sovereignty, enterprises must bring external models into a secure, self-controlled environment where data is utilized purely as context, and internal behavioral traces are locked in a continuous, non-leaking loop.

The Evolution of Work: Agentic Development Environments (ADEs)

  • The Shift to Agentic Workflows: Enterprise work is transitioning from simple, single-prompt chat interactions to long-running, autonomous agentic loops (such as Microsoft's Scout and Autopilot) where users delegate a high-level intent and receive finished pull requests (PRs) or completed workflows.
  • Managing Cognitive Load with ADEs: As users deploy hundreds of agents simultaneously, managing them via command-line chats creates extreme cognitive fatigue.
      • The Agentic Development Environment (ADE): Microsoft is shifting the developer workspace (via GitHub) to an ADE, featuring a visual "Canvas" and inbox-style UI that allows humans to easily micro-steer macro-delegated agents.
  • Governance via "Agent 365": To manage safety, identity, and security across millions of enterprise agents, Microsoft has extended its core security suite:
      • Identity & Containment: Using Entra to provide unique identities to agents, and sandboxing their execution environments to prevent unauthorized network or file system access.
      • Observability: Using Defender and Purview to automatically audit reasoning traces and auto-label agent-created data.
      • Runtimes & Asserts: Incorporating hard "asserts" into developer runtimes (via Azure AI Foundry) to programmatically keep agents within defined operational guardrails.
  • Cognitive Coverage: A critical new human skill where workers create deductive evaluations and quizzes to verify, understand, and learn from what their autonomous agents have executed.

Strategic Efficiency: Silicon, Supply Chains, and Token Management

  • The Frontier Model Fallacy: A major operational dictum for modern enterprises is: *Don't use frontier models for non-frontier problems.*
      • Task-Specific Optimization: Repeatable, deterministic workflows (e.g., processing retail trade promotions) should not use expensive general-purpose frontier models. Instead, firms should use smaller, highly optimized models (like Microsoft's Phi-3/MAI 5B) trained on specialized traces to outperform prompted frontier models at a fraction of the cost.
  • The AI Supply Chain: Within the next year, CEOs will pivot heavily toward mapping and securing their "AI supply chain" to ensure they can uniquely point to and compound their own digital value.
  • Custom Systems and Silicon: Microsoft is optimizing its infrastructure vertically from civil engineering to silicon to maximize the "electrons-to-tokens" conversion efficiency:
      • Maia: Custom silicon co-designed with Azure and OpenAI models to optimize large-scale, synchronous training and inference workloads.
      • Cobalt: An Arm-based CPU designed specifically to reduce latency and power consumption for the highly complex call patterns of coding and agentic runtimes.
      • Data Center Power Delivery: Re-engineering power distribution directly from high-voltage AC/DC inputs to the silicon level to minimize distribution loss.

Geopolitics, Sovereignty, and the Humanist Imperative

  • Sovereign AI and Comparative Advantage: Rather than implementing protectionist digital firewalls, countries should leverage David Ricardo’s theory of comparative advantage.
      • Local Infrastructure: True sovereignty comes from providing clean, cheap energy to power data centers, enabling local public and private sectors to "hill-climb" on top of frontier models rather than falling behind the global frontier.
  • Securing Social License: The tech industry must move past abstract promises and deliver tangible, localized benefits to earn public trust.
      • Proof Points: Data centers must directly benefit local tax bases, schools, and water/grid infrastructure.
      • Scientific Breakthroughs: AI's societal permission increases when applied to tangible fields like medicine and biology. (e.g., *GigaPath*, a digital pathology foundation model developed by Microsoft Research, UW, and Providence to radically lower the cost of evaluating immunotherapy for tumors).
      • Board Transition: Reid Hoffman announced he will transition off the Microsoft Board after 10 years to focus in "founder mode" on Manas, an AI startup applying computational chemistry and AI to cancer therapeutics.
  • A New Virtuous Cycle: Mirroring historical insights from Pope Francis’s humanist teachings and the industrial era, Nadella asserts that long-term global prosperity requires a harmonious alignment between moral philosophy, public policy, competitive markets, and scientific breakthroughs.

Notable Quotes

"AI is not a technology. It's the future of the firm." — Satya Nadella
"One of the dictums I have is: don't use frontier models for non-frontier problems." — Satya Nadella
"If humanity can get past their bounded rationality and say, 'No, no, no, let's maybe we can change the course,' then the first step would be to accept that that possibility exists." — Satya Nadella
So, what I think we have not yet conceptually gotten right, or a shared understanding of, is what is this future of work going to look like? If you're a tech CEO, you have to be deep inside of what's the tech stack. AI is not a technology; it's the future of the firm. One of the dictums I have is: don't use frontier models for non-frontier problems. I think in the AI age, that is going to be everything, right? I think I would be very surprised, Reid, if we were sitting here a year from now, if the world is not completely turned on, "What does my AI supply chain look like?" I couldn't be more delighted to introduce a special episode of Possible with Satya Nadella, the Chairman and CEO of Microsoft. Actually, one of the things that's great, Satya, about filming this here is that it reminds me of the earliest days when we were talking about Microsoft and LinkedIn. That's right. Because we did one of our very, very early conversations here in the Greylock office. So, it's just awesome to be back. That is sort of because I grew up in Hyderabad in India, and Urdu is sort of in the air. Some of the Urdu poets, both modern and from the 17th or 18th century, were just extraordinary. In particular, you know, there's this poet who goes by Ghalib as his name, and he's just extraordinary. Or even a modern poet like Faiz. Obviously there was English, there was Hindi, which was the national language, and then we had Sanskrit—basically like Latin here, I guess. Then we had the local language, Telugu, which is my mother tongue, but we also had Urdu and Persian. In fact, when we would break for what was called the second language, my second language was Sanskrit. But I had classmates who would go to Persian, Urdu, Telugu, or Hindi, and it was fascinating. Well, among the experiences, I'm experiencing a little bit of language envy. A joke that I like is: what do you call a person who speaks three languages? Trilingual. Two languages? Bilingual. One language? American. And unfortunately, I resemble this joke. All right, so we're just coming out of Build, which was amazing. Let's kind of actually start by going back in history. You know, Microsoft's first product was a BASIC interpreter. And 50 years later, here at Build, you framed the company's future around what others can build with AI. So what is the new BASIC interpreter in the AI era? No, that's a great, great one. I think that is the core of the DNA of the company, right, which is: what can others build, and therefore what should we build? Those are sort of the two questions that we always ask ourselves. And in the AI era, to me, the answer is that the BASIC interpreter is the hill climbing machine, right? Because when you bring in all this AI, what is it? It is basically taking an objective, an outcome, an eval that you have, and learning how to achieve that by using data and some reinforcement reward. And so the entire conference was not about, "Hey, here is a new frontier model." It was about helping every developer, every company—whether it's an AI-native startup or an enterprise—build their own hill climbing machine so that they can operate at the frontier, right? So I think that that's it. The idea of getting very clear about the evals and the objectives that you care deeply about, and knowing how to evaluate them, is in some sense the most critical part. Keeping that is the new IP, because everything else is pretty mechanical. Knowing what is the set of data that you want to train a model on and how you reward it is probably where the next level of IP gets created. Well, let's dig into a couple of different areas here, because you and I have had a number of conversations about Microsoft's strategy. One of them is this question about how enterprises keep the advantage and integrity of their own data. Microsoft is the most natural company in the entire world to do this. So say a little bit about how enterprises should be thinking about this, like, "We need our own frontier intelligence, but we also need to maintain control over our data." Yeah, see, that I think is the question, right? This economy is going to be shaped going forward by both human capital and, let's call it, this token capital, right? That is true for Microsoft, that is true for a new startup, and that's going to be true for any bank that's been in existence for 100 years. It doesn't matter. All of us will now need to compound the returns of the interplay between human capital and token capital. So if you frame it that way, then one of the most important things is not just thinking of data in its aggregate sense. See, what is the tacit knowledge of an enterprise or a firm? It's the unique ways that you are able to operate, pass judgment, and have taste. All that is the tacit knowledge, mostly captured today in the human capital and some digital artifacts. So now, when it comes to AI, the model in some sense is able to extract that tacit knowledge through human trajectories and encode it in a set of weights. What I claim is that every enterprise now needs to be more mindful about that interplay of humans and their digital estate working together. Those trajectories essentially train the models that they should keep as IP, versus leaking it. Because if you leak it, it's a one-way door. You're done, in some sense, with what is unique to you, what you may have spent 100 years building. We don't even know how to articulate it, right? Nobody has a line item on their balance sheet called "tacit knowledge," but we take it for granted because we have human capital. Now I believe that can leak. And in fact, it is leaking, right? If you look at how the model companies learn, they're essentially setting up these gyms with rewards and employing employees who worked at your company previously. I mean, that should tell you everything of what should not be happening. So that's one of the fundamental reasons why we want this paradigm to change. You welcome the models to come in, but they should hill climb inside a machine that you control. Your data is your context that you feed the model. You collect these traces or trajectories of how work gets done between humans and agents inside the enterprise, but you keep a continuous loop of that and you're not letting it leak. That, I think, is the fundamental operation. Yep. And actually, the related parallel to the tacit knowledge is a conversation you and I had at the Microsoft board, which was, "What is the future role of AI employees?" Right? Part of the question is to say, "Hey, we're going to provision not just tools to amplify human work for AI companies, but we're also going to provision at least specialist employees." But the challenge with that is that one of the things that really matters to enterprises is that your employees embody a lot of tacit knowledge on how and what to do. Do you want that in other companies and provisioned in other places? What's your and Microsoft's view of the future of work when it comes down to how to think about AI also as maybe specialist employees? Yeah. I think that that's right. There are two ways I come at this. Let's take one analogy and I'll come back to it. If somebody in the early '80s had come to us and said, "You know what? There are going to be 4 billion typists who are going to wake up every morning and start typing," we would have said, "What for?" It makes no sense. We had a typist pool, we had a slide pool, and we were fine with that. Except we invented this completely new thing called knowledge work, where everybody was typing and creating digital artifacts. So what I think we have not yet conceptually gotten right, or have a shared understanding of, is what is this future of work going to look like? Let's take Microsoft: we have 200,000 employees and we have, let's say, 2 million agents or 20 million agents, all in a loop. What is happening? What is the tacit knowledge that gets created? What are the artifacts that are going between agents, and between agents and humans? All that's now going to be played out over the next multiple years. You can see early forms of this in coding, right? It's a great place to observe the social change. Think about how we started in a good old IDE and said, "Hey, AI is in there and it's doing code completion." That itself was useful and easy to understand. We've always had spelling correction, and IntelliSense was there in VS Code 15 years ago, and it just got better. Then we said, "Instead of going out of band to a browser and searching Stack Overflow, you can now bring all the coding knowledge to a chat session." Well, that was also easy to understand, and you still were in the IDE with the chat. Then we said, "Okay, you now have reasoning models and some preliminary agent loops." And so you can assign tasks. We had Agent Mode, right? So you not only had chat, but you could give it small tasks, see them complete, accept what they did, and insert it. Then came the big breakthrough of total autonomy and agentic loops working for long periods of time where you could literally fire-and-forget. You could assign a high-level intent, it would go off and do the full PR, and you would accept the PR. So that transition is what I think is going to happen across all work. And now we are seeing it with Copilot; we now have chat and we have co-work, and at Build, we announced something called Scout and Autopilot. So what is happening in coding will happen even in knowledge work. Interestingly enough, one of the things we launched at Build in GitHub was a new feature called Canvas. As we've all gotten so good at using these coding agents, the biggest challenge we now have is having 100 CLI sessions open where we are trying to operate these 100 agents. And now the cognitive load on managing this is so high, right? I mean, think about it: it's a linear chat session in a command line and you have 100 of them. So guess what? We are back to an IDE. We have a new IDE and we give it a fancy name: it's called an ADE, which is an Agentic Development Environment. That's what the new GitHub app is. The GitHub app looks like an inbox, except it's an inbox of agents that are working across all the repos, allowing me to do the micro-steering of the macro-delegation I gave them. It's a complete UI for the agents to deal with me and for me to deal with agents. So we introduced this new feature called Canvas in GitHub where, for example, sometimes it's easy to have a Kanban board visualization as a way to run down your PRs. It is very useful both for agents and me to interact, versus just a simple chat session. So I think that type of innovation is how work will change. In fact, one of the other fascinating lines of AI research will be: models getting much more tuned to learning how to stay the course and understanding human preferences in steerability, because that's what I want. I want models that not only do instruction following, but are also really steerable. When you have that, that's when you have confidence in them. So I think the work of the future is about the tacit knowledge that gets created by this interplay. Just like knowledge work got created because of digital artifacts and human capital, the new era will be this AI capital and human capital working together, creating in some sense the digital artifacts. Well, what do you think are the things that people miss about how important it is to build out additional structure for enabling enterprises? One of them is obviously enabling the human's Canvas, ADEs, etc. But what about, for example, notions of what security is in an agentic world? Oh, that's a great point. So, to your point, one is the experience layer; clearly that's important. Another one, which we referenced earlier, is: we need this hill climbing machine as a concept that needs to be instantiated. But the third is the manageability of it and the security of it, starting with observability. One of the first things we did, which we talked about yesterday, is something called Agent 365. I need to have an inventory. If there are 20 million agents at Microsoft, I first need to know: what are these agents, what are they doing, and what are their reasoning traces? They need to be fully inspectable and fully auditable. And by the way, when the agents also have this other attribute where they execute, run, and generate code, you need the environment in which they're executing code—with perhaps file system and network access—to be governed by policy. Therefore, you now need to give them identities, you need to give them sandboxes, and you need to set policies to govern them. We built this entire system called Agent 365, which has extended Entra so that you have an identity, extended Defender so that you have security, and extended Purview so that you can automatically label the data being created for data protection. So I think security, containment, manageability, and observability are the ways we will have confidence around these agents. The one other thing I'd say, Reid, that was salient in our developer conference was how important it is when you're building these long-running agents. In programming languages, we have always had programming models for understanding the verifiability of a program and its execution at runtime. One of the attributes of these long-running agents that we added to Foundry was something called asserts. This allows us to assert what the boundaries are, right? So instead of talking about guardrails as just a classifier of some form, you really need to now have the ability during execution to ensure that the execution path does not go off the rails. There's a lot of engineering sophistication now that's emerging as we build out the platform, the runtime, the security layer, the management layer, and the observability layer. So let's go to the role of the CEO. I think Fortune recently described you as acting like a startup CEO inside of Microsoft's AI teams. But the more general question, because I think a lot of CEOs seek technology strategy advice from you, is what do you think CEOs should be doing around AI? AI brings a kind of refounding moment to lots of companies because of its nature. So what's your advice to them for how they should engage? It's a great question because I've been thinking about this. Clearly, if you're a tech CEO, you have to be deep inside of what's the tech stack. There's no way you can be a tech CEO and not essentially have a fundamental worldview on where the future is going. Long before it's conventional wisdom, you have to pass judgment on where the company is going. That's our industry's fairly binary transition. I think there's no hope if you don't have the CEO leading from the front and taking the shot on goal, knowing that these are fairly harsh transitions. But the interesting thing now is that, up to now, if you were not a tech CEO, you just needed to be a great CEO—which means you need to be great at banking, healthcare, or whatever it is—and you could always have great partners and technology advisors. Now, I'm changing my prior on that. I'm noticing what's happening, and I don't think the rest of the industry and the CEO community has broadly woken up to this. I'm actually perplexed by it because they're still pretty happy doing a press release with a tech company, saying, "Yeah, I've got my AI strategy," and pointing to eight agents they built and some outcome. But AI is not just a technology. It's the future of the firm, right? It would be like saying, "Oh, I don't know anything about the human capital in my firm." My perspective is that you have to now get a deep understanding of what's your token capital. Can you answer that question? Can you concretely say, "Based on all the work that happened across all of our operations, we were able to translate that into a set of knowledge that is somehow now part of my token capital, whether it's context, skill, or weights in a model"? It doesn't need to be any one thing, but you need to be able to identify it clearly as something that you own, you control, you created, and you've put in place a system to let compound. I think that is going to be probably the toughest change. This change is unlike moving to the mobile phone, PC, or cloud eras, where all I needed to do was have an IT department that dealt with a bunch of vendors and did some smart things to reduce cost or improve efficiency. It should start there—I'm not saying that's not the place—but this is about what happens structurally in your industry when AI basically knows everything that it needs to know about being in your industry. If you start there, then I think you will start understanding that this is not just tech; it is really about a fundamental change to the firm. And one of the things that comes to mind regarding the nature of the firm is that you've done a great job in leading Microsoft through acquisitions and partnerships, right? Whether it's LinkedIn, obviously something close to our hearts, GitHub, or OpenAI. Part of what I think Microsoft leads the entire world on is how you build trust in these other ecosystems. In the world of AI, that's going to be extremely important, especially as the firm changes. So what are some of the lessons you've learned, and the things that you are advocating for, in order to build and maintain that trust? Yeah, it's so key because, in some sense, I've always thought about what is long-term stable from a Microsoft perspective. What is long-term stable is for us to be a tools and platform company where we fundamentally are defined by the amount of value that gets created on top of the platform, which should far exceed anything that is captured in the platform. That's the only way to have stability. If you do that, then you have trust because the customer or partner knows that it's not a zero-sum game—especially not the kind of game-theoretic zero-sum where a company subsidizes something for a few years only to then eat your lunch. I've always felt like that is not the way. The way to think about it is to be very principled that you're a platform company, and that you will really live and die by your ability to create success on top of the platform. When they're successful, you're going to be successful. That is the equation that builds trust and long-term stability for both sides. I think in the AI age, that is going to be everything. I would be very surprised, Reid, if we were sitting here a year from now and the world has not completely turned on, "What does my AI supply chain look like?" where it is helping me as a firm compound the returns of AI that I can uniquely point to as my value. This is crucial in a world where we know that these learning systems by definition don't have boundaries. They have to have boundaries because of structures of markets, society, and other mechanisms. So we've talked a lot about chatbots and code, which have just seen amazing, next-level progress. What are some of the next frontiers for LLMs over the next few years? Well, talking about that, I know one of the things that you have gotten very, very excited about—which frankly, I'm excited about too, but has also caused you to examine where you spend your time—is what's happening in science. In fact, the company that I know you started with Sid, Manas, is a great example of what has happened in coding and knowledge work now happening in science. I wish we had done it in the reverse order, because the social permission for AI would be so much higher if we had started with some nice discoveries in science that were having great societal benefits. People would have really thought of this as a thing that is deeply helpful. I'm really excited for what you're doing there, and maybe you want to talk about that. Well, yeah. One of the things I realized over the last month was that we're seeing such progress with Manas. Actually, we've got an internal description of "Move 37 for chemistry" because we're seeing chemistry that might actually take shots at really interesting cancers and other things. It's very early, and these things take a while, but the fact is we are already beginning to see results. We've got some of the best computational chemists in the world, and they looked and said, "That's very interesting. We've never seen it before, and that might work." So Sid, Ujjwal, and I were talking about this, and I said, "Look, I think I need to get back to founder mode in terms of how to do this." I need to be able to focus on this. Part of the conversation you and I had this week is to say, "Okay, it's been 10 years on the Microsoft board, which has been a huge honor and pleasure, not just with LinkedIn, but obviously OpenAI, GitHub, and a whole bunch of things." But at the end of the year, I should really transition to founder mode. We'll always be working together, but it's time to dig back into the company. First of all, it's been such a privilege to have you on the Microsoft board and work with you, obviously through all the partnerships and LinkedIn. You will definitely be missed on the Microsoft board, though I know we'll stay very connected. But I'm also excited for you when you say you're going back into founder mode; that means I'm even more curious about what you're going to be building. As you rightfully captured, this is the moment where having the impact of these technologies more broadly felt in society is the need of the hour. No, exactly. Let's come back to questions around the patterns of thinking that happen with AI. The way I roughly think about these things is that you've got an alien intelligence—a different version of intelligence—that has been built to heavily mimic human intelligence, which creates a whole bunch of utility for us. But its patterns of reasoning are not the same as ours. People frequently encounter that and think, "Oh, that's scary." But actually, while you have to pay attention to make sure there's alignment, it's also wonderful because it enables new things. That is like the kinds of chemistry we're doing; humans hadn't discovered this chemistry before Manas. What are your thoughts about this pattern of reasoning and how it can amplify human capability? It's a great question. I've not thought that deeply about how it improves our induction and deduction, but being in this cognitive loop with an AI is fascinating because my exploration space is changing. In coding, one of the most fascinating things a colleague introduced me to is a new skill in GitHub Copilot called cognitive coverage. What he's done is, whenever an agent does some work for me, just like we had test coverage, we now have this new concept called cognitive coverage where we as humans are going to learn from what it did. It essentially creates a quiz. I think of learning from it as matching my ability to form a deductive understanding of what the agent did. To your point, it is mimicking—an imitation game on one side. But that imitation game does create a need for our ability to deductively understand it, which is probably one of the more important human skills we have to develop. I'm fascinated by cognitive coverage. The agents are working and they have to be aligned on one side, but on the other side, what is new will be us knowing that we have cognitively covered what the AI did. And I think that point intersects with your earlier one about token capital and AI capital. Part of the skill set for the modern human knowledge worker is saying, "Okay, how do I strategize on the use of AI, Canvas, and orchestration, but also within capital allocation bounds?" We have seen people go crazy spending lots of tokens in ineffective ways, almost like the bad Pentagon days of buying toilet seats for a million dollars. So we have to put our thinking together with the cognition and token management. What do you think about the skill of blending cognitive coverage with token intelligence in terms of how we amplify the outcomes? It's a great point. I've been thinking about what becomes more valuable in the age of token abundance. One is what you referenced: how to use tokens becomes very valuable. Whoever figures out how to use tokens more efficiently for an outcome that matters in the world is going to get ahead, by definition. How does one build the intuition for it? This is where the evals and the rubrics come in. It is fascinating how much time needs to be spent setting up the rubric and the scoring dimensions so they really capture the high taste that only you can define. If you do that, then you can be token efficient. The other side of token efficiency shows up in examples we shared at Build. Let's say you are a retailer or a packaged goods company, and one of the things you do is handle transactions around trade promotions. Right now, you get all these claims from retailers, humans process them, and you could easily automate that using an agentic workflow with a frontier model. But one of my dictums is: don't use frontier models for non-frontier problems. This is not a frontier problem; you're not trying to discover some new material science. This is a repeatable, deterministic workflow that can benefit from intelligence. You can take a smaller model like MAI-5B and use traces to hill climb and outperform even a prompted frontier model. That is token efficiency. This type of human understanding of both the limits and the characteristics of the system becomes a high premium at this stage. I agree. Another thing that is important for people to grasp is sovereign AI. We've been in a lot of conversations about this, and there is concern in other countries. Given how we've gone in depth about company sovereignty—their information, their employees—what are the parallels between what we're doing for companies and how countries should think about engaging and trusting in the AI future? This is interesting because one of the reasons I have pivoted to companies is that companies exist all over the world. That's the good news. When countries think abstractly about sovereignty, they can make big mistakes that ultimately erode their comparative advantage, which is naturally embodied in their local commercial activity and company growth. Preserving that is the best form of sovereignty. Sometimes people think, "If only I had a firewall, or if all the data was resident." I'm not saying those are not considerations, but this is about making sure you have a thriving economy. That means having companies that thrive in a token economy and are able to build that IP. Having partnerships with outside companies that give you that ability is much more important than suddenly falling behind the frontier. Therefore, policymakers have to think about infrastructure. After all, tokens are electrons on one end and tokens on the other. You want to be the cheapest, best, environmentally sound producer of those electron-to-token conversion machines called data centers. That is going to be very important for every country to prioritize. Of course, countries should also prioritize having their own semiconductor production, which are very valid things to do. But beyond that, the most important thing is to remember what David Ricardo was right about: countries have a comparative advantage, and now they need to amplify that using AI. The best approach is to make sure your small businesses, large multinationals, and public sector are operating at the frontier. The worst thing is to fall off the frontier in the name of sovereignty, because then you fall behind. At the same time, being solely dependent on one frontier model means you are not sovereign. The way to solve this is to say, "No, we will use models to hill climb on our own, one firm at a time, and as an economy in the aggregate." That, I think, is the equation. The mention of silicon is a good transition for both companies and countries. We are building Maia and Cobalt, but also partnering closely with NVIDIA and AMD. What is the strategic job of Microsoft's own silicon, and what might companies or countries learn from that? It is interesting if you think about it. If you look at what NVIDIA, AMD, or Intel have done, they have built general-purpose technology. When I look at what we are doing with GPUs today, we are of course using them for model training and model inference, but we are also accelerating other workloads. One of the exciting announcements at Build was using NVIDIA GPUs to accelerate our Fabric data warehouse. With agentic workloads, we need more performance on everything. Because of their general-purpose nature, GPUs—like NVIDIA with CUDA—can be used as a programming model to accelerate a variety of workloads. In fact, we are using older NVIDIA chips to accelerate workloads, which works out well economically. It is smart for us to take a fleet and use it over a lifetime, using it not only for cutting-edge AI but also to make older workloads perform better. That is great for NVIDIA and great for us. But that also speaks to what is happening with new workloads. The new workloads of AI are data-parallel, synchronous workloads for training and inference, as well as new agent runtime workloads. These have very different call patterns and are highly specific. They didn't exist at any scale three years ago, and now they exist at scale. So it behooves us to start thinking about this even before we get to the semiconductors, in how we build data centers. Our civil engineering is influenced, as well as our cooling systems and mechanical systems. We are working on DC-to-AC distribution to make sure electrons and hundreds of kilowatts go straight to the silicon without losses. We are trying to minimize power distribution losses in our data centers so we can optimize to the nth degree for these massive-scale workloads. That is what we are doing with Maia. Maia is being co-designed with our models and OpenAI models to get the best performance out of them. We are designing Cobalt, which is our Arm-based compute core, and we are designing it for things like the agentic traces of GitHub. The call pattern of a coding agent is quite different from human apps or asynchronous human apps, so we are optimizing to get massive latency and performance gains. We are going to be a systems company that continues to optimize for new at-scale workloads while using general-purpose technology from our partners to maximize utility. That flexibility is what is really valuable. Countries should think about that too. If you think there is only one answer—if we said everything is Maia and everything is Cobalt—that would not be the right thing for Microsoft. We are open to innovation from the outside, we will innovate inside, we will benchmark everything, and we will be principled about it for better economics. Yes, because creating efficient capital token factories that create human prosperity and enabling many companies to do it is the goal. On a different thread, one of the things I know from our conversations is that you are very thoughtful about children in the modern age. We are creating products for tutors, co-workers, and so on. What should we be doing as an industry to help children elevate the right way while keeping them safe? What are some of the principles Microsoft and you are thinking about to support the next generation? It's a great question. There are obviously important things we have to do around child safety. When any digital technology comes out, we have to think about this as a first-class issue. There are AI safety issues around cyber, bioweapons, and alignment, but there is also child safety. We want to make sure we address the challenges of current chatbots and their conversations with children. We must be very mindful and ensure that we are not stripping children of the agency they need to interact with these systems on their own terms, rather than being persuaded by them. Those are very important things. The other thing you are pulling on, which I think is super important, is what it even means to be a child in a world with an abundance of tokens. How should learning happen? How should we inspire children, and what is their ability to enter a new pedagogical system? The anxiety that I had growing up around learning is an artifact of the scarcity of opportunities and scarcity of good teaching—things which may not be true going forward. So, one of the first things is creating a learning environment where students and children don't develop phobias for math or science from an early age. Everyone is by definition curious, but contact with the world often erodes that innate curiosity and confidence. We can develop that further by giving them the ability to explore without anxiety, knowing that expertise will always be there in abundance. It's really your cognitive coverage of that expertise that is at a premium. If someone had said that to me as a five-year-old, I think I would have approached life very differently. How we as a society create the necessary conditions for that is going to be very important. I know from our intense conversations that this is one of our key responsibilities as a tech industry. We cannot abrogate it. Given the ubiquity of AI technology, we have to be responsible for the next generation of humanity. Exactly. We have to think about the unintended consequences from day one, build in safety guardrails, and democratize the great advantages of this new technology. I also think there needs to be structural change. I don't think education can remain exactly the same, or that we will continue to value the exact same credentials. Something has to change. One of the things we haven't had a chance to talk about yet, which is very natural in this context, is the Pope's encyclical. I thought it was a magnificent act of leadership on the part of the Church. Pope Francis actually got me engaged in helping them talk about AI ten or eleven years ago, so the Church has been amazingly front-footed on this. Part of what didn't surprise me at all in the encyclical was its humanism. Some people might think, "Oh, it's about religion and how you pray," but actually, the parts of the Church I've interfaced with have been deeply humanist. Do you have any reflections on the Pope's encyclical? To see the Pope weigh in on this is incredible, and from what I understand, there is historical precedent for it. I believe the Pope at the time of the Industrial Revolution also weighed in on the condition of labor and what should be done. To have the Pope come out in defense of what all of us deeply care about—which is human dignity and human agency in the age of AI—is so important, and I'm glad he has advocated for it. The other side of this to me is how society responds. When I think about some of the greatest ways technological advances were harnessed to create great prosperity, the story of the West is pretty unbelievable. There is a beautiful book by Joel Mokyr and a couple of other authors called *Two Paths to Prosperity*, which describes the thousand-year history of China and the West. I'm paraphrasing here, but fundamentally, some of the cultural and societal constructs in the West necessitated a real change in how society was organized so that it could take advantage of the scientific and industrial revolutions. That, in fact, defined the modern world. We now need a similar coming together from moral philosophy. The Pope basically said this needs to be the moral philosophy that guides us going forward. You combine that with the market, democracy, and the scientific-technological revolution. If we can get into a virtuous cycle where morality, scientific breakthroughs, the political system, and the markets reinforce each other, then we will have abundance and many stakeholders will benefit. If we don't, we are going to lose social permission. I think a narrow understanding of the success of the West as just a technological breakthrough is incorrect; it was an unbelievable coming together of multiple forces in a virtuous cycle. It also had really bad parts to it, and we have to avoid repeating those. The Pope wrote about those bad parts and how we can advocate for a positive cycle, which I thought was beautifully captured. I thought it was great that there was a focus on how we keep humans at the center, elevate human dignity, and address the needs not just of wealthy countries but of the entire world. It is a great beacon for how we should think. Exactly. I think deeply about that. For example, I have always cared deeply about the global South finally having its moment for real catch-up growth. There is a real danger in the age of AI that this convergence growth could slow down or go the other way. We have to consider what the global structures are. Someone sitting in Palo Alto might think it doesn't impact them, but there's no such thing. We share this planet, and our destinies are much more tied than we think. Last question before we get to rapid fire. As we both know, there is a lot of AI backlash in the US and Europe. Part of how I have tried to address it is with books like *Superagency*, saying, "This is an opportunity to gain agency." The transition will be difficult, but you ultimately have to embrace the transformation. If you do it with forethought and lean into it, it can be greatly helpful. What do you think we should do to help people in the US and the West understand why embracing AI is so important? While there are genuine issues, backlash, and work transitions that are very real, how do we help people see that this can be an important and positive part of their future? I think, Reid, at least for now, I have come to the conclusion that there need to be real, tangible, practical, well-understood outcomes that speak for themselves. What has happened—and this is one of those places where, quite frankly, our industry is responsible for how we talk about it—is that we say things like, "All economic opportunity will go away for knowledge workers," or "White-collar jobs are gone," and then add, "And I'm excited about building that technology." Why would anyone want you to be successful? I wouldn't want you to be successful either; it makes no social sense. When you have someone at a college commencement getting booed because they mentioned AI, it means we have crossed over to a point where people don't believe us, and rightfully so. Therefore, what do we need to do? Now is the time to do the hard work. The hard work is, if you're building a data center, let's make sure that that community believes that this data center is great for them—it's for their tax base, for their community efforts, their real estate value, their schools, their water use, right, and their electricity prices. It can't be again like, "Oh, I said something." No, it has to be real, right? That's kind of the way we earn social permission. Same thing, I would say, with employment. We can't abstractly even say, "Hey, lump of labor fallacy, there's always going to be new jobs." What are the new jobs? What are the wages of the new jobs that I can now go apply for, train myself for, and how do I really start a new company or what have you, right? I think unless we really get clear... Or the third thing, which we unpacked a lot: every firm needs to participate in the frontier ecosystem. It's not like, "Oh, I'm just a feeder of data to some foundation model." Like, that is—talk about sovereignty and dignity both being lost, right, simultaneously, right, for countries, communities, and companies. So I think now we have to go all the way and say, "Okay, this is a positive sum. This is, in fact, the—you know, challenges of some of the technology, we're going to really actively work it. We can articulate the tangible benefits." And this is where, again, what you will do at Manus and others are also going to be very, very helpful, because the world needs more proof points that this technology is ultimately helping the human condition and our societies broadly, not narrowly. Yeah, no. AI for humanity. So, you don't have to answer rapidly, but we ask the same questions of all our guests. So the first one is: Is there a movie, song, or book that fills you with optimism for the future? It's this "Two Paths to Prosperity", alright, because I find—the reason why I like that is because it's a good call to action for the next thousand years. What's the path to prosperity? There was a blueprint. At least a part of the world got it right in the last thousand. Can we now, as an entire planet, get it right for the next thousand? I think that this is where some of our very best work has to be done. Agreed. What's a question that you wish people would ask you more often? Oh, that's a good... I would love for people to ask me what am I not excited about. Because I'm excited about a lot of things. But I'm not excited, for example, about us losing permission on AI by sort of saying all the wrong things or doing the wrong things, even, and not having a complete thought on how to truly have a positive sum construct. Yeah, 100%. So where do you see progress or momentum outside of your industry? Given that Microsoft powers a lot of the world's industry, but, you know, it could be robotics, AI, etc., where do you see progress that inspires you? I mean—I mean, to some degree, yeah, the work in bio. I mean, if I think about it, right, what are the most complex systems that we have to have a better understanding of? It is human biology. So anything, any tool that can help humans take care of humans is probably the thing that will be awe-inspiring. There's this one thing I recently came across, right, which is for immunotherapy. I believe there's a test that is, you know, basically a complex, costly test that figures out whether a particular immunotherapy will work on that tumor or not. And so Providence and some researchers at UW and Microsoft Research came together and built this thing called GigaPath, which is a cool model which basically is a simulation of that test. It reduces the cost of what could get done only at sort of—it took a lot of time and a lot of money, and now can be done by any tertiary sort of hospital in any city. That type of economic availability of medicine and medical practice, I think, is just a breakthrough. So, last question. Can you leave us with a final thought on what you think is possible to achieve if everything breaks humanity's way in the next 15 years, and what's our first step? I think if everything breaks our way, I've always gone back to, like, what's the dream of the world compounding at 10% GDP growth? Right? That is what can happen, right? I mean, one thought experiment is: if the Industrial Revolution had reached all corners of the world at the same time and every country could express their comparative advantage fully, right, that's the maximalist positive sum construct. Can we do it? Because we're kind of captive to this sort of what we will describe as, "Hey, that's not how the world works; history is about dominance and dominant powers." And I'm not saying we will defy all that, but the bottom line is, you know, since you asked me to dream, I'm dreaming. If humanity can get past their bounded rationality and only sort of say, "Oh, history repeats itself and we're only never going to get better than, you know, fighting these wars and what have you," and say, "No, no, no, let's—maybe we can change the course," then the first step would be to accept that that possibility exists. That is what I would say is what will get us down the path. Otherwise, we're going back to relitigating, "Oh, we are—let me look at history and then just try to match." Beautiful dream. Satya, always a pleasure, and I'll see you next week at the board meeting. Thank you so much, Reid. Thank you so much. This is awesome. This is such a fun conversation. Yes, exactly.
